Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Ash'ari Proof for the Vision of Allah
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the Ash'ari proof for the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Ash'ari claim that every existent can be seen with the five senses.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the overgeneralization that every existent is capable of being sensed.

In this passage, after affirming the possibility of seeing Allah based on transmitted texts and sound reason, Ibn Taymiyya criticizes the argument of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and his followers. They claimed that every existent can be seen by intellectual proofs and even that the five senses can be attached to Allah. Ibn Taymiyya dismisses this as known necessarily to be false. This lesson dissects that critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari claim
Read the line where Ibn Taymiyya describes their position: 'they claimed that every existent can be seen by intellectual proofs' and 'the five senses can be attached to Him.' This is the target doctrine.
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He says 'this is known necessarily to be false in the view of the majority of rational people.' He considers the claim self-evidently absurd.
3
Reflect on the absurdity
Can we attribute smell, taste, and touch to Allah? The claim implies that Allah could be tasted, smelled, etc., which is theologically problematic.
4
Recognize Ibn Taymiyya's alternative
He himself affirms the possibility of seeing Allah but via proper proofs (textual and rational). He does not deny the vision; he rejects the faulty reasoning.

Source Text

The Unseen is Sensed: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Intellectualizing the Ghayb
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes ghayb as sensed, criticizes theologians' claim that all existents are sensible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the unseen is sensibly perceived and distinguish it from the theologians' claim that all existents are sensible.

Core Idea
The unseen is not abstract but sensed — just not by our current senses.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the assumption that the unseen (ghayb) is merely intellectual. He insists that matters of the unseen — the afterlife, angels, the vision of Allah — are in fact sensible (mahsus), but they are perceived after death or by divine permission in this world. The distinction between 'unseen' and 'witnessed' is relative to our current sensory capacity, not an intrinsic difference. In this passage, he also critiques the theologians who wrongly claimed that every existent can be perceived by the senses.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's central claim about the unseen
Find where he says the unseen matters are 'sensed, witnessed, and felt' (محسوسة تُشاهَد وتُحَس). Note that he asserts they are not merely intellectual.
2
Understand the relativity of ghayb vs shahada
He states that 'a thing being absent or present is relative to us.' The distinction is not intrinsic. What is ghayb now may become shahada later.
3
Distinguish purely intellectual entities
Ibn Taymiyya says whatever is purely intellectual (لا يمكن أن يُشهد) exists only in the mind. Angels and Allah are not like that; they can be witnessed.
4
Analyze the critique of the theologians
He rejects the claim that every existent can be sensed by the five senses, calling that false necessarily. Identify the specific theologians he names (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari) and what they claimed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أكمل وأعظم مما نشهده نحن في هذه الدار. وتلك أمور محسوسة تُشاهَد وتُحس ولكن بعد الموت وفي الدار الآخرة. ويمكن أن يشهدها في هذه الدار من يختصه الله بذلك، ليست عقلية قائمة بالعقل. ولهذا كان الفرق بينها وبين الحسيات التي نشهدها أن تلك «غيب» وهذه «شهادة». قال تعالى: ﴿ الَّذِينَ يُؤْمِنُونَ بِالْغَيْبِ ﴾ [سورة البقرة : ٣]. وكون الشيء غائباً وشاهداً أمرٌ إضافي بالنسبة إلينا. فإذا غاب عنا كان «غيباً»، وإذا شهدناه كان «شهادة». ليس هو فرقاً يعود إلى ذاته تعقل ولا تشهد ولا تُحسن. بل كل ما يعقل ولا يمكن أن يُشهد بحال فإنما يكون في الذهن. والملائكة يمكن أن يُشْهَدوا ويُرَوا. والرب تعالى يمكن رؤيته بالأبصار، رؤية الملائكة والرب تعالى والمؤمنون يرونه يوم القيامة وفي الجنّة، كما تواترت النصوص في ذلك عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم، وافق على ذلك سلف الأمة وأئمتها. وإمكان رؤيته يُعلم بالدلائل العقلية القاطعة. لكن ليس هو الدليل الذي سلكه أهل الكلام، كأبي الحسن وأمثاله، حيث ادعوا أن «كل موجود يمكن بالدلائل رؤيته». بل قالوا: ويمكن أن تتعلَّق به الحواس الخمس السمع، والبصر، والشم، والذوق، واللمس. فإن هذا مما يعلم فساده بالضرورة عند جماهير العقلاء.
More complete and greater than what we witness in this abode. Those matters are sensed, witnessed and felt, but after death and in the Hereafter. And it is possible that they be witnessed in this abode by one whom Allah distinguishes with that; they are not intellectual matters subsisting in the intellect. Hence the difference between them and the sensed things we witness is that those are 'unseen' and these are 'witnessed'. Allah Almighty said: 'Those who believe in the unseen' (Surah al-Baqarah: 3). And a thing being absent or present is relative to us. So if it is absent from us, it is 'unseen', and if we witness it, it is 'witnessed'. It is not a difference returning to its essence such that it is neither intellected nor witnessed nor sensed. Rather, whatever is intellected and cannot possibly be witnessed at all is only in the mind. And angels can be witnessed and seen. And the Lord Almighty can be seen with eyes; the angels and the Lord Almighty are seen by the believers on the Day of Resurrection and in Paradise, as the texts have been transmitted successively from the Prophet (peace be upon him), and the predecessors of the Ummah and its imams agreed upon that. And the possibility of seeing Him is known by conclusive intellectual proofs. But it is not the proof that the theologians followed, like Abu al-Hasan and his likes, where they claimed that 'every existent can be seen by proofs.' Rather, they said: It is possible for the five senses to perceive Him: hearing, sight, smell, taste, and touch. For this is known to be false necessarily in the view of the majority of rational people.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage critiques the theological claim that the unseen (ghayb) is purely intellectual and that all existents can be sensed. Ibn Taymiyya begins by asserting that the unseen matters of the Hereafter are actually sensed (mahsus), but after death or by divine permission in this world. They are not merely intellectual (aqliyya). The distinction between ghayb and shahada (witnessed) is relative to us, not an intrinsic difference. What is purely mental cannot be sensed at all. Angels and even Allah can be seen in the Hereafter according to transmitted texts. The theologians (mutakallimun) like Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari wrongly claimed that every existent can be seen by proofs and that the senses can perceive Allah; Ibn Taymiyya says this is known to be false by necessity. The passage defends the sensory reality of the unseen against intellectual reductionism.

Key Terms
غيب
شهادة
عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, how do the unseen matters (ghayb) differ from merely intellectual matters?
A) They are purely rational and cannot be sensed.
B) They are sensibly perceived in the Hereafter or by divine permission.
C) They are only known by the intellect, not by revelation.
D) They are identical to the witnessed world.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the view that the unseen is purely intellectual; it is sensed in the Hereafter or by divine permission, and the distinction from the witnessed world is relative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a contemporary theological claim that treats the unseen as purely symbolic or intellectual. Write a brief critique in the style of Ibn Taymiyya, using his distinction between ghayb and shahada.
?
In a debate about whether angels can be seen in this world, use Ibn Taymiyya's argument to show that the unseen is not essentially non-sensible, but only hidden from our current senses.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Theologians and Philosophers on Bodies and Atoms
Ibn Taymiyya critiques theologians' errors on accidents, atoms, and causation, and philosophers' errors on composition and divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific errors Ibn Taymiyya attributes to theologians and philosophers regarding bodies, atoms, and accidents, and explain his comparative assessment of their severity.

Core Idea
Both theologians and philosophers err, but philosophers' errors in divine matters are worse.

This passage is a critical overview where Ibn Taymiyya lists errors from two groups. The theologians mistakenly claim accidents cannot persist, bodies are composed of indivisible atoms, and that God acts without wisdom. The philosophers err in claiming bodies are composed of matter and form. Ibn Taymiyya also warns that these theological errors gave ammunition to philosophers, who then thought their own views were the only truth. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for evaluating the debate between kalam and falsafa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the theological errors
Scan the passage for the specific claims Ibn Taymiyya attributes to 'some theologians' (بعض المتكلمين). Look for phrases like 'إن الأعراض يمتنع بقاؤها' (accidents cannot persist) and 'إن الأجسام متماثلة' (bodies are similar) and 'مركبة من الجواهر المنفردة' (composed of indivisible atoms). List them.
These errors are listed at the beginning of the passage.
2
Identify the philosophers' error
Find where Ibn Taymiyya mentions the philosophers' error on composition: 'مركبة من المادة والصورة العقليين' (composed of matter and intellectual form). Note that he calls it equally an error.
3
Understand the causal link
Notice how Ibn Taymiyya says the theologians' errors gave power to the philosophers (سلَّط أولئك المتفلسفة). Reflect on the dynamic: theological mistakes enable philosophical criticism.
4
Compare the severity
Read the sentence where Ibn Taymiyya states that the philosophers' error in ilahiyyat, nubuwwat, ma'ad, and shara'i' is greater than the theologians' error. But in natural sciences, philosophers may be more correct.
This is a key comparative judgment.
5
Extract the key distinction
Ibn Taymiyya does not reject all natural science; he specifically critiques the claim of burhan (demonstration) yielding certain universals in nature. Note that this is a refinement of his position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا من أغاليط بعض المتكلمين، كغلطهم في قولهم: «إن الأعراض يمتنع بقاؤها، وإن الأجسام متماثلة، وإنها مركبة من الجواهر المنفردة التي لا تقبل قسمة، ولا يتميز منها جانب من جانب. فإن هذا غلط . وقولُ المتفلسفة إنها مركبة من المادة والصورة العقليين أيضاً ،غلط، كما قد بسط هذا كله في غير هذا الموضع . وكذلك غلط من من المتكلمين وادّعى أن الله لم يخلق شيئاً بسبب ولا لحكمة، ولا خص شيئاً من الأجسام بقوى وطبائع . وادعى أن كل ما يحدث فإن الفاعل المختار الذي يخص أحد المتماثلين بلا تخصيص أصلاً يُحدِثه. وأنكَرَ ما في مخلوقات الله وما في شَرْعه من الحكم التي خَلَق وأَمر لأجلها . فإن غَلَط هؤلاء مما سَلَّط أولئك المتفلسفة، وظنوا أن ما يقوله هؤلاء وأمثالهم سبب تسليط دين المسلمين، أو قول الرسول وأصحابه . ولهذا كانت مناظرة ابن سينا وأمثاله في كتبهم لمبتدعة أهل الكلام فعامة مُناظرة ابن سينا هي للمعتزلة، وابن رُشد للكلابية. وكانوا إذا بينوا فساد بعض ما يقوله مبتدعة أهل الكلام يظنون أنه لم يبق حق إلا ما يقولونه هم، وذلك بالعكس. وليس الأمر كذلك، بل ما يقوله مبتدعة أهل الكلام فيه خطأ مخالف للشرع والعقل . والخطأ فيما تقوله المتفلسفة في الإلهيات والنبوات، والمعاد، والشرائع، أعظم خطأ المتكلمين . وأما فيما يقولونه في العلوم الطبيعية والرياضية فقد يكون صواب المتفلسفة أكثر من صواب مَنْ رَدَّ عليهم من أهل الكلام فإن أكثر كلام أهل الكلام في هذه الأمور بلا علم، ولا عقل، ولا شرع. ونحن لم نقدح فيما عُلم من الأمور الطبيعية والرياضية، لكن ذكرنا أن ما يدعونه من «البرهان» الذي يفيد علوماً يقينية كلية بالأمور الطبيعية ليس كما يَدَّعونه، بل غالب عادات تقبل التغير، ولها شروط وموانع وهم لا يريدون بالقضايا البرهانية الواجب قبولها إلا ما يكون لزوم المحمول منها للموضوع لزوماً ذاتياً لا يقبل التغير.
This is among the errors of some theologians, like their error in saying: 'Accidents cannot persist, bodies are similar, and they are composed of indivisible atoms which are not divisible and no side is distinguished from another.' For this is an error. And the philosophers' statement that they are composed of matter and intellectual form is also an error, as has been fully expounded elsewhere. Likewise the error of those among the theologians who claimed that Allah did not create anything for a cause or for wisdom, nor distinguished any body with powers and natures. And he claimed that every event is produced by the free agent who distinguishes one of two similar things with no specification at all. And he denied the wisdoms in Allah's creations and in His law for which He created and commanded. For the error of these gave power to those philosophers, and they thought that what these and their likes say is the cause of the empowerment of the religion of Muslims, or the statement of the Messenger and his Companions. Hence the disputation of Ibn Sina and his likes in their books was with the innovators of the theologians; generally Ibn Sina's disputation is with the Mu'tazila, and Ibn Rushd's with the Kullabiyya. And when they showed the corruption of some of what the innovating theologians say, they thought that no truth remains except what they themselves say, and that is the opposite. And it is not so; rather, what the innovating theologians say contains error contrary to the Law and reason. And the error in what the philosophers say in theology (ilahiyyat), prophethoods (nubuwwat), the afterlife (ma'ad), and the laws (shara'i') is greater than the error of the theologians. As for what they say in the natural and mathematical sciences, the truth of the philosophers may be more than the truth of those who refuted them from the theologians, for most of the theologians' speech in these matters is without knowledge, reason, or law. And we have not critiqued what is known of natural and mathematical matters, but we have mentioned that what they claim of 'demonstration' that yields certain universal sciences in natural matters is not as they claim; rather, most habits are subject to change, and they have conditions and obstacles, and they do not intend by apodictic propositions that must be accepted except what has the predicate's entailment of the subject as an essential entailment that does not admit change.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a critical summary of errors by both theologians (mutakallimun) and philosophers (mutafalsifa). Ibn Taymiyya lists specific theological errors: the claim that accidents cannot persist, that bodies are self-similar, and that bodies are composed of indivisible atoms. He also notes the philosophers' error of composition from matter and form. He then criticizes theologians who deny divine wisdom and causality, claiming God acts arbitrarily without cause or purpose. He argues that these theological errors empowered the philosophers, who then attacked theologians and wrongly concluded that the truth is only with philosophy. In reality, both groups have error, but the philosophers' errors in divine matters (ilahiyyat) are more severe. However, in natural sciences, philosophers may be more correct than theologians. Ibn Taymiyya then clarifies that he does not reject all natural science, but he critiques the claim of 'demonstration' (burhan) that yields universal certainties in nature, arguing that natural habits are changeable and depend on conditions.

Key Terms
أعراض
جواهر منفردة
مادة وصورة
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, whose errors are worse in divine matters (ilahiyyat)?
A) The theologians' errors are worse.
B) The philosophers' errors are worse.
C) Both are equally erroneous.
D) Only the theologians err in divine matters.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between the errors of theologians and philosophers, deeming the latter's errors in divine matters more severe while acknowledging their possible correctness in natural sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate with a philosopher who claims that 'water boils at 100°C is a universal necessary truth', use Ibn Taymiyya's critique to explain why this is not a burhani certainty but a habit ('adah) subject to change.
?
Identify which of the following is a theological error according to this passage: (a) bodies are composed of indivisible atoms, (b) accidents persist, (c) God creates without wisdom. Write a short response explaining your choice.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Claim to Burhan in Natural Sciences
Ibn Taymiyya critiques theologians' errors on accidents, atoms, and causation, and philosophers' errors on composition and divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the philosophers' claim that burhan yields certain universal knowledge in natural matters, and identify his alternative view of natural regularities as habits ('adat) subject to change.

Core Idea
Burhan does not yield certain universal truths in nature; natural regularities are habits that can change.

In the latter part of this passage, Ibn Taymiyya directly addresses the philosophers' conception of demonstration (burhan). He argues that what they call apodictic proof in natural sciences does not deliver the certainty they claim, because natural regularities are not necessary but habitual, and they depend on conditions and obstacles. This critique is important for understanding the limits of reason in natural theology and the basis for miracles.

Guided Path
1
Locate the critique of burhan
Find the section starting with 'ونحن لم نقدح' (And we have not critiqued) and read to the end of the passage.
2
Identify the two opposing claims
Philosophers claim burhan yields certain universal sciences in natural matters. Ibn Taymiyya says it is not as they claim (ليس كما يَدَّعونه).
Contrast the two views.
3
Understand his reasons
He says 'غالب عادات تقبل التغير' (most habits are subject to change) and that these matters have 'شروط وموانع' (conditions and obstacles). This means natural regularities are not necessary but contingent.
4
Identify the implied positive view
Natural regularities are not necessary entailments but customary outcomes that can be altered by the Creator. This preserves the possibility of miracles.
5
Connect to Islamic theology
This critique undermines the philosophical argument for the necessity of natural causation and supports the Ash'ari view that God directly creates effects.
Example: fire does not burn cotton by nature, but by God's will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to Ibn Taymiyya, why does the philosophers' claim of burhan in natural sciences fail to yield certain universal knowledge?

Refutation of Multiple Substances in a Human
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the claim that a concrete human is composed of multiple distinct substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the metaphysical claim Ibn Taymiyya refutes and explain why he considers it self-evidently false based on proper conceptualization.

Core Idea
A human is one substance, not a bundle of distinct substances for each attribute.

Ibn Taymiyya takes aim at a philosophical claim that a concrete human being is composed of multiple distinct substances, one for each attribute like body, sensitive, rational, etc. He argues that once you properly conceive what this claim means, its falsehood becomes immediately obvious. This lesson unpacks his critique and the epistemological move he uses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find in the Arabic the claim that substance is composed of multiple substances corresponding to attributes like 'body', 'sensitive', 'growing', etc. Note the list of attributes.
2
Understand the entailment of distinct substances
The claim entails that the substance which is 'sensitive' is not the same as the substance that is 'rational' or 'body'. That is, each attribute corresponds to a separate substance.
3
Grasp Ibn Taymiyya's reply
He says that once you properly conceptualize (tasawwur bi al-sura) this claim, its falsity (fasad) becomes known. He appeals to immediate intellectual intuition (badiha).
4
Recognize the epistemological move
Ibn Taymiyya is not providing a detailed argument; he is claiming self-evidence. This is a typical method to reject philosophical distinctions that violate common sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أن يقال: إن «الجوهر مركب من أعراض أو مركب من جواهر أحدها «جسم»، والآخر «حساس»، والآخر «نام»، والآخر «متحرك بالإرادة»؛ وإن هذا الإنسان المعين فيه جواهر متعدّدة بتعدّد هذه الأسماء ؛ وإن الجوهر الذي هو «الحساس» ليس هو الذي هو متحركاً بالإرادة، ولا الذي هو «جسم»، ولا الذي هو ناطق» ولا «الناطق» هو «الحساس» ؛ فهذا مما يعلم فساده بعد تصوره بالصورة.
As for the claim that 'substance is composed of accidents or composed of substances, one of which is 'body,' another 'sensitive,' another 'growing,' another 'moving by will'; and that this particular human contains multiple substances according to the multiplicity of these names; and that the substance that is 'sensitive' is not the one that is moving by will, nor the one that is 'body,' nor the one that is 'rational,' nor is 'rational' the 'sensitive'—this is something whose falsity is known after its proper conceptualization.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a categorical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of a specific metaphysical claim: that a concrete individual human is composed of multiple distinct substances corresponding to each attribute (body, sensitive, growing, rational, etc.). He lists the attributes as jism (body), hassaas (sensitive), naami (growing), mutaharrik bi al-irada (moving by will), and natiq (rational). The claim, he says, entails that the substance of 'sensitive' is different from 'moving by will' and from 'body' and from 'rational,' and 'rational' is not identical to 'sensitive.' Ibn Taymiyya states that once one properly conceptualizes (tasawwur) this claim, its falsity (fasad) becomes evident—it is a self-evident falsehood. This is a typical Ibn Taymiyyan move: he appeals to immediate intellectual intuition (badiha) to reject a philosophical proposition that violates common sense. The implication is that the logicians' analysis of humans into multiple substantial components is absurd; a human is a single substance with multiple attributes, not a collection of distinct substances.

Key Terms
الجوهر
التصور بالصورة

The Philosophers' Contradictory Tawhid: Denying and Affirming Attributes
Ibn Taymiyya exposes logical contradictions in the philosophers' concept of a simple Necessary Existence with multiple identical attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical contradiction in the philosophers' concept of a simple Necessary Existence with multiple identical attributes.

Core Idea
The philosophers' tawhid contradicts itself: denying attributes while affirming identical ones.

Ibn Taymiyya exposes a devastating contradiction at the heart of the philosophers' concept of God. They claim He is stripped of all positive attributes, yet they attribute to Him intellect, delight, love, and claim each is identical to the other and to God. By examining this passage, you will learn how to identify internal contradictions in theological claims using necessary knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' two contradictory claims
First, note that they deny all positive attributes (life, knowledge, power). Then, find where they affirm attributes like intellect, delight, love. These are contradictory.
The contradiction is between the general denial and the specific affirmations.
2
List the attributes they affirm
They say God is intellect, intelligible, intelligence; delightful, delighted, delight; lover, beloved, love. List these three triads.
Each triad is a set of three things.
3
Note the identification claims
They claim each attribute is the other (delight is love, love is intellect) and each attribute is the described (knowledge is the knower).
This collapses the distinction between attributes and subject.
4
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's argument from necessary knowledge
He says that the mere conception of these statements, correctly understood, yields necessary knowledge of their falsity. No further proof is needed.
This is an epistemological argument: the inconsistency is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
توحيد واجب الوجود عند الفلاسفة
وكذلك «الواحد الذي يصفون به واجب الوجود»، وأنه مجرد عن جميع / الصفات الثبوتية ليس له حياة ولا علم ولا قدرة ولا كلام ويقولون مع ذلك : /٣١٤ هو عاقل ومعقول وعقل، ولذيذ وملتذ ،ولذة وعاشق ومعشوق وعِشق. ويقولون: إن كل صفة هذه الصفات من الأخرى، فاللذة هي العشق، والعشق هي العقل، والعقل هي اللذة. ويقولون : إن كل صفة من هذه الصفات هي الموصوف، والعلم هو العالم، واللذة هي الملتذ، والعشق هو العاشق فهذا ونحوه من أقوالهم في صفات «واجب الوجود» مما إذا تصوّره المتصوّر تصوراً صحيحاً كان مجرد تصوره يوجب العلم الضروري بفساده . وقد بسطنا الكلام عليه، وبينا ما ألجأهم إلى القول بهذا وكلامهم في «التركيب»، وبينا أن أكثر الفلاسفة المتقدمين قبل أرسطو وكثير من المتأخرين كأبي البركات صاحب المعتبر وغيره لا يقولون بهذا، بل ردوا على من قاله وأصل هذا كله ما ادعوه من أن إثبات الصفات تركيب ممتنع. وهذا أخذوه عن المعتزلة، ليس هذا من كلام أرسطو وذويه.
The Tawhid of the Necessary Existence according to the philosophers. And likewise the 'one' that they describe the Necessary Existence with, and that it is abstract from all positive attributes, having no life, knowledge, power, or speech, yet they say at the same time: it is an intellect, an intelligible, and intelligence; delightful, delighted, and delight; lover, beloved, and love. And they say that each of these attributes is the other: delight is love, love is intellect, intellect is delight. And they say that each attribute is the described: knowledge is the knower, delight is the delighted, love is the lover. That and similar statements of theirs about the attributes of the 'Necessary Existence' are such that if one conceives them correctly, the mere conception necessitates necessary knowledge of their falsity. And we have elaborated on this, and shown what compelled them to say this and their discussion of 'composition,' and shown that most of the earlier philosophers before Aristotle and many of the later ones like Abu al-Barakat (author of al-Mu'tabar) and others do not say this; rather they refuted those who said it. The origin of all this is their claim that affirming attributes is an impossible composition. They took this from the Mu'tazila; this is not from the speech of Aristotle and his followers.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a sharp critique of the philosophers' concept of tawhid (divine unity) regarding the Necessary Existence (Wajib al-Wujud). Ibn Taymiyya lays out their paradoxical position: on one hand, they claim God is abstract from all positive attributes (no life, knowledge, etc.), yet on the other hand, they attribute to Him multiple qualities like intellect, intelligible, intelligence; delight, delighted, delight; lover, beloved, love. Moreover, they claim each attribute is identical to the other and to the subject (e.g., knowledge is the knower). Ibn Taymiyya argues that this is a clear contradiction that any rational person can see as false upon proper conception (tasawwur). He then provides historical context: earlier philosophers before Aristotle and later ones like Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi (author of al-Mu'tabar) rejected this view. He traces the origin of this idea to the Mu'tazila, who argued that affirming distinct attributes implies composition, which is impossible for God. It was the Mu'tazila, not Aristotle, who originated this line of reasoning. This passage demonstrates Ibn Taymiyya's method: using self-evident reason and historical precedent to refute a philosophical doctrine, and exposing its foreign (non-Aristotelian) roots.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
التركيب
المعرفة الضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) From the Mu'tazila
C) From Abu al-Barakat
D) From the earlier philosophers

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' tawhid is self-defeating because it simultaneously denies and affirms attributes, a contradiction known by necessary reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Quranic Analogy for Resurrection from Plant Growth
Presents the fourth Quranic analogy for resurrection and asserts Quran's superiority over philosophical proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the analogical argument from the Quranic verses linking plant revival to resurrection.

Core Idea
The Quran uses a visible natural cycle to prove an invisible divine act.

Ibn Taymiyya presents the fourth Quranic analogy for the possibility of resurrection, drawing on the observable process of plant growth after rain. He cites two verses (7:57 and 35:9) that directly compare reviving dead land with the resurrection of the dead. This lesson explores how the Quranic analogical argument works and why it is considered a strong proof in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Quranic verses cited
Locate the verses (7:57 and 35:9) in the Arabic passage. Notice how they describe the process: winds, rain, dead land, plants, then explicitly link it to resurrection.
The verses use the same Arabic root for 'bringing forth' (ikhraj) and 'giving life' (ihya).
2
Extract the source of the analogy (al-asl)
The source is the observable event: reviving dead land through rain and producing plants. Identify the key elements: dead land, rain, and the resulting plant growth.
3
Extract the target of the analogy (al-far')
The target is the resurrection of the dead (al-nushur). Identify the parallel: God's power to give life to dead land is used to argue for the possibility of giving life to dead bodies.
4
Identify the common factor ('illa) linking source and target
The 'illa is God's absolute power to give life, which is not limited to any particular type of matter. If God can give life to dry earth, He can give life to decomposed bodies.
The 'illa is implicit—the verses draw a direct analogy without stating a separate cause.
5
Evaluate the strength of the analogy
Consider that the analogy is based on repeated observable experience (rain reviving land). The Quran presents it as a self-evident argument. Note that Ibn Taymiyya treats it as a certain proof (yaqini).
In the Islamic tradition, this analogy is considered an argument of istidlal, not formal qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة : وتارة يستدل على إمكانه بخلق النبات، كما في قوله: ﴿ وَهُوَ الَّذِي يُرْسِلُ الرِّيحَ بشرا بين يَدَى رَحْمَنِهِ حَتَّى إِذَا أَقَلَتْ سَحَابًا ثِقَالًا سُقْتَهُ لِبَلَو مَيْت فَأَنزَلْنَا بِهِ الْمَاءَ فَأَخْرَجْنَا بِهِ مِن كُلِّ الثَّمَرَاتِ كَذَلِكَ يُخْرِجُ الْمَوْتَى لَعَلَّكُمْ تَذَكَرُونَ ﴾ [سورة الأعراف : ٥٧]، وكما في قوله : ( وَاللَّهُ الَّذِي أَرْسَلَ الرِّيحَ فَتُثِيرُ مَحَابًا فَسُفْتَهُ إِلَى بَلَدٍ مَيْتِ فَأَحْيَيْنَا بِهِ الْأَرْضَ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهَا كَذَلِكَ النُّشُورُ) [سورة فاطر: ۹] . وقد بسطنا الكلام على هذا وأمثاله في غير هذا الموضع، وبين أن ما عند أئمة القرآن أكمل النظار - أهل الكلام والفلسفة - من الدلائل العقلية على المطالب الإلهية فقد جاء القرآنُ بما فيها من الحق، وما هو أكمل وأبلغ منها على أحسن وجه، مع تنزهه عن الأغاليط الكثيرة الموجودة عند هؤلاء فإن خطأهم فيها كثير جداً، ولعل ضلالهم أكثرُ مِن هداهم، وجهلهم أكثر من علمهم . ولهذا قال أبو عبد الله الرازي في آخر عمره في كتابه «أقسام اللذات» : لقد تأمّلتُ الطرق الكلامية والمناهج الفلسفية، فما رأيتُها تشفي عليلاً ولا تُرْوِي غليلاً، ورأيتُ أقرب الطرق طريقة القرآن - أقرأ في الإثبات الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى . [سورة طه : ٥]، ﴿ إِلَيهِ يَصْعَدُ الْكَلِمُ الطَّيِّبُ ﴾ [سورة فاطر : ١٠]؛ وأقرأ في النفي: ﴿ لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْ [سورة الشورى : ۱۱]، ﴿ وَلَا يُحِيطُونَ بِهِ عِلْمًا ﴾ [سورة طه : ۱۱۰] .
Fourth: And sometimes He argues for its possibility by the creation of plants, as in His saying: 'And it is He who sends the winds as glad tidings before His mercy until, when they have carried heavy rainclouds, We drive them to a dead land and send down rain therein and bring forth thereby all kinds of fruit. Thus will We bring forth the dead, that you may remember' [Quran 7:57], and as in His saying: 'And Allah is the One who sends the winds, and they stir up clouds, and We drive them to a dead land and give life thereby to the earth after its death. Thus is the resurrection' [Quran 35:9]. And we have elaborated on this and its like in another place, and clarified that what the imams of the Quran—the perfect examiners among the people of kalam and philosophy—possess of rational proofs for divine matters, the Quran has brought the truth contained therein, and what is more complete and more excellent than them in the best manner, while being free from the abundant fallacies found among those people, for their errors therein are very many, and perhaps their misguidance is greater than their guidance, and their ignorance more than their knowledge. Hence Abu Abd Allah al-Razi said at the end of his life in his book 'The Divisions of Pleasures': 'I have contemplated the theological methods and the philosophical methodologies, and I have not seen them cure the ill nor quench the thirsty. I have seen the nearest method to be the method of the Quran—I read in affirmation: 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' [Quran 20:5], 'To Him ascend good words' [Quran 35:10]; and I read in negation: 'There is nothing like Him' [Quran 42:11], 'And they do not encompass Him in knowledge' [Quran 20:110]'.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth of four Quranic demonstrations for the possibility of resurrection, using the analogy of bringing plants to life after rain. Ibn Taymiyya provides two Quranic verses (7:57 and 35:9) that draw a parallel between reviving dead land with rain and resurrecting the dead. After presenting this evidence, he asserts that the Quranic method is superior to the rational methods of theologians and philosophers because it contains the truth embodied in their proofs while being more complete and free from the many fallacies that plague human reasoning. To reinforce this, he quotes Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's late-in-life confession that theological and philosophical paths do not satisfy, and that the Quranic method—affirming God's attributes as He describes and negating any likeness—is the closest. This serves to undermine the authority of the rationalist tradition Ibn Taymiyya is criticizing.

الإمكان
النشور

B) The analogy of reviving dead land with rain and bringing forth plants
C) The categorical syllogism that every living being dies
D) The necessary existence of a creator

Key Takeaway
The Quranic analogy for resurrection uses the observable cycle of plant growth to logically prove the possibility and reality of life after death.

Ibn Taymiyya's Epistemological Critique of Philosophical Theology
Presents the fourth Quranic analogy for resurrection and asserts Quran's superiority over philosophical proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's argument for the superiority of Quranic evidence over philosophical reasoning and explain the significance of al-Razi's quote.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya uses al-Razi's own testimony to delegitimize philosophical theology.

After presenting the Quranic analogy for resurrection, Ibn Taymiyya makes a sharp epistemological claim: the Quranic method is superior to the rational methods of theologians and philosophers. He supports this by quoting Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, a leading philosopher-theologian, who at the end of his life admitted that philosophical paths do not satisfy. This lesson unpacks Ibn Taymiyya's critique and its basis in the authority of a former opponent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of Quranic superiority
Find where Ibn Taymiyya says the Quran brings truth that is 'more complete and more excellent' than philosophical proofs, and that it is free from fallacies.
He contrasts the Quran with the abundant errors of philosophers.
2
Analyze the grounds for the claim
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the Quran contains the truth of rational proofs but is superior because it is complete and free from fallacy. He does not provide a detailed logical demonstration here but relies on the sheer number of errors in philosophy.
His argument is epistemological: the Quran's method is inherently sounder.
3
Examine the quotation from al-Razi
Al-Razi confesses that philosophical and theological methods do not 'cure the ill or quench the thirsty', and that the Quranic method of affirmation and negation is the nearest. Note his specific examples: affirming God's attributes without likeness and negating any comparison.
Al-Razi was a master of both kalam and philosophy, so his admission carries weight.
4
Evaluate the rhetorical strategy
Ibn Taymiyya uses the authority of a philosophical opponent to support his position. This is a form of argument from authority (ijma' or shahada). Consider whether this strengthens or weakens his critique.
The critique is not purely logical but also rhetorical and based on personal testimony.
5
Assess the internal validity of the critique
Ibn Taymiyya's own reasoning here is not a formal syllogism; it is an epistemological argument from the completeness of Quranic evidence. The al-Razi quote serves as inductive support. The critique is valid as a statement of comparative merit, but it does not formally refute specific philosophical arguments.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn Taymiyya quote Fakhr al-Din al-Razi in this passage?
A) To show that al-Razi agreed with his own views
B) To prove that the Quranic method is superior using the testimony of a former philosopher
C) To refute al-Razi's earlier philosophical works
D) To demonstrate the logical consistency of kalam

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses al-Razi's late-life testimony to argue that the Quranic method of affirming God's attributes without likeness is epistemologically superior to philosophical theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Based on the passage, write a summary of Ibn Taymiyya's three main points against philosophical theology. Then evaluate: does the al-Razi quote conclusively prove the superiority of the Quranic method? Why or why not?
?
Compare Ibn Taymiyya's critique here with his broader critique of logic in 'al-Radd 'ala al-Mantiqiyyin'. How does the use of an authority figure (al-Razi) fit into his overall argumentative strategy?

Prophetic Teaching: Rational and Transmitted Proofs
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that prophets taught both rational and transmitted proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's two-fold characterization of prophetic teaching and explain how it refutes the narrow conception of prophethood.

Core Idea
Prophetic teaching is not merely transmitted reports; it includes rational proofs for divine matters.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the logicians asserts that the prophets taught both rational proofs ('adilla 'aqliyya) and transmitted reports ('adilla sam'iyya). Many speculative thinkers mistakenly thought prophetic teaching was only about transmitted knowledge, but Ibn Taymiyya argues that the prophets themselves clarified intellectual proofs and informed about the unseen. Understanding this comprehensive view is essential for properly locating the role of reason in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misconception
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states what many speculative thinkers think about prophetic teaching. What is that view? He says 'as many of the speculative thinkers think' – that teaching is confined to mere reports.
Look for the phrase 'كما يظنه كثير من النظار' (kama yazunnuhu kathīr min al-nuẓẓār).
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's positive claim
What does he say prophetic teaching actually includes? Identify the two types of proofs mentioned and note the key phrase 'جامع للأدلة العقلية والسمعية جميعاً' (encompasses both intellectual and transmitted proofs).
The prophets completed both matters for people: rational proofs for divine matters and details of the unseen.
3
Analyze the Quranic support
Ibn Taymiyya quotes three verses from Surah Ghafir (40:56, 35, 83). For each, determine how it relates to his argument about arrogance and rejection of proofs. These verses highlight that those who dispute without authority are arrogant and will not attain truth.
The verses serve as evidence that the opponents' rejection stems from arrogance, not lack of proof.
4
Assess the epistemological implications
Based on this passage, what is the relationship between rational proofs and transmitted reports in prophetic teaching? Does one supersede the other? Ibn Taymiyya says the prophets completed both – they are complementary, not exclusive.
This refutes the false dichotomy that prophetic teaching is either purely rational or purely transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأنبياء ـ صلوات الله عليهم - كَمَّلوا للناس الأمرين، فدلوهم على الأدلة العقلية التي بها تعلم المطالب الإلهية التي يمكنهم علمهم بها بالنظر والاستدلال، وأخبروهم مع ذلك من تفاصيل الغيب بما يَعْجزون عن معرفته بمجرد نظرهم واستدلالهم . / وليس تعليم الأنبياء - صلوات الله عليهم - مقصوراً على مجرد الخبر، كما يظنه كثير من النظار . بل هم بينوا من البراهين العقلية التي بها تعلم العلوم الإلهية ما لا يوجد عند هؤلاء ألبتة. فتعليمهم - صلوات الله عليهم - جامع للأدلة العقلية والسمعية جميعاً بخلاف الذين خالفوهم فإن تعليمهم غير مفيد للأدلة العقلية والسمعية مع ما في نفوسهم من الكبر الذي ما هم ببالغيه، كما قال تعالى : ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يُجدِلُونَ فِي عَايَتِ اللهِ بِغَيْرِ سُلْطَنٍ أَتَنَهُمْ إِن فِي صُدُورِهِمْ إِلَّا كِبَرُ مَّا هُم بِبَلِغِيهِ فَاسْتَعِذْ بِاللَّهِ إِنَّهُ هُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ ﴾ [سورة غافر : ٥٦]، وقال: ﴿ الَّذِينَ يُجَدِلُونَ فِي عَايَتِ اللهِ بِغَيْرِ سُلْطَانٍ أَتَنْهُمْ كَبُرَ مَقْتًا عِندَ اللهِ وَعِندَ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كَذَلِكَ يَطْبَعُ اللَّهُ علَى كُلِّ قَلْبٍ مُتَكَتِرٍ جَبَّارٍ ﴾ [سورة غافر : ٣٥]، وقال تعالى : ﴿ فَلَمَّا جَاءَتْهُمْ رُسُلُهُم بِالبَيِّنَتِ فَرِحُوا بِمَا عِندَهُم مِّنَ الْعِلْمِ وَحَافَ بِهِم مَّا كَانُوا بِهِ يَسْتَهْزِعُونَ ﴾ [سورة غافر : ٣]. ومثل هذا كثير في القرآن وقد بسطنا القول فيه في بيان دَرْءِ تعارض الشرع والعقل.
Then the prophets, peace be upon them, completed both matters for people: they guided them to the intellectual proofs by which divine matters that they can know through examination and inference are known, and they informed them along with that of details of the unseen that they are incapable of knowing by mere examination and inference. And the teaching of the prophets, peace be upon them, is not confined to mere reports, as many of the speculative thinkers think. Rather, they clarified intellectual proofs by which divine sciences are known, which these people have not at all. So their teaching, peace be upon them, encompasses both intellectual and transmitted proofs, unlike those who opposed them, for their teaching is not beneficial for intellectual and transmitted proofs, along with the arrogance in their souls that they will not attain, as He said: 'Indeed, those who dispute concerning the signs of Allah without any authority having come to them, there is nothing in their breasts but arrogance, which they will never attain. So seek refuge in Allah. Indeed, He is the Hearing, the Seeing.' [Surah Ghafir: 56], and He said: 'Those who dispute concerning the signs of Allah without any authority having come to them, it is greatly hateful to Allah and to those who believe. Thus does Allah seal upon every arrogant tyrant heart.' [Surah Ghafir: 35], and He said: 'But when their messengers came to them with clear proofs, they rejoiced in what they had of knowledge, and they were encompassed by what they used to ridicule.' [Surah Ghafir: 83]. And similar to this is frequent in the Qur'an, and we have expanded on it in the book 'Dar' ta'āruḍ al-shar' wa al-ʿaql.'
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage refutes the misconception that prophetic teaching is only about transmitted reports. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the prophets taught both rational proofs and revealed knowledge. He begins by stating that the prophets completed both matters for people: they showed them rational proofs for knowing divine matters, and also informed them of unseen details. Then he explicitly states that prophetic teaching is not confined to mere reports, contrary to what many speculative thinkers assume. They clarified rational proofs that their opponents do not possess. Therefore, prophetic teaching encompasses both rational and transmitted proofs. He then quotes three Quranic verses to support his point and refers to his larger work on the harmony between reason and revelation.

Key Terms
الأدلة العقلية
الأدلة السمعية
تعليم الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only transmitted reports
B) Both rational and transmitted proofs
C) Only rational proofs
D) Only moral teachings

Key Takeaway
Prophetic teaching is comprehensive, encompassing both rational proofs for divine matters and transmitted reports of the unseen.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Philosophers' Sciences
Ibn Taymiyya recounts the philosophers' division of sciences and critiques the divine science as purely mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of sciences in the philosophers' classification and explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique that their divine science is purely mental.

Core Idea
The philosophers' highest science is only mental; the truly Highest is Allah.

Ibn Taymiyya engages with the philosophers' classification of sciences into natural, mathematical, and divine. He explains their criteria of abstraction from matter, then turns the tables: only the natural and parts of mathematical sciences correspond to external realities. The divine science, meant to be the highest, is shown to be a mental construct. This lesson unpacks that critique and its implications for distinguishing true knowledge from speculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of sciences
Find in the passage the three divisions: natural (tabi'i), mathematical (riyadi), and divine (ilm ma ba'd al-tabi'a). Note how the philosophers rank them from lowest to highest.
2
Understand the abstraction criterion
The philosophers classify based on abstraction from matter: natural does not abstract, mathematical abstracts mentally but not externally, divine abstracts both mentally and externally. Grasp this criterion.
3
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's evaluation
Ibn Taymiyya claims only natural and some mathematics have external existence. Why? Because mathematical quantities exist mentally, and divine science deals with 'universal existence' which has no external referent.
4
Examine the critique of divine science
The subject of divine science is 'universal existence' (al-wujud al-kulli). Ibn Taymiyya argues this exists only in the mind. Contrast this with the Quranic verse mentioning the 'Highest' (al-A'la) which refers to Allah, who truly exists externally.
5
Connect to the Islamic alternative
Ibn Taymiyya redirects from speculative metaphysics to revealed knowledge: the true Highest is Allah, known through His signs and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بناء جعلهم علومهم ثلاثة أنواع. ولهذا لما كانوا يتصورون في أذهانهم ما يظنُّون وجوده في الخارج جعلوا علومهم ثلاثة أنواع : أدناها عندهم الطبيعي، وهو ما لا يتجرَّد عن المادة، لا في الذهن ولا في الخارج، وهو الكلام في الجسم وأحكامه وأقسامه. وأوسطها «الرياضي»، وهو ما يتجرَّد عن المادة في الذهن لا في الخارج، مثل علم الحساب والهندسة. فإنه لا في الخارج ذو مقدار إلا جسماً، ولكن يجرَّد المقدار في النفس. وأعلاها عندهم ما يسمونه «علم ما بعد الطبيعة» باعتبار الاستدلال العِلْمي، وما قبل الطبيعة باعتبار الوجود العيني، وقد يسمونه «العلم الإلهي»، ويسمونه «الفلسفة الأولى» و «الحكمة العليا»، وهو ما تجرَّد عن المادة في الذهن والخارج. وإذا تأمل الخبير بالحقائق كلامهم في هذه الأنواع لم يجد عندهم بمعلومات موجودة في الخارج إلا القسم الذي يسمونه «الطبيعي» وما يتبعه من «الرياضي». وأما الرياضي المجرَّد في الذهن فهو الحكم بمقادير ذهنية لا وجود لها في الخارج. والذي سموه «علم ما بعد الطبيعة»، وهو ما جرَّدوه عن المادة في الذهن والخارج، إذا تُدبّر لم يوجد فيه علم بمعلوم موجود في الخارج، وإنما تصوروا أموراً مقدرة في أذهانهم لا حقيقة لها في الخارج. ولهذا كان منتهى نظرهم وآخر فلسفتهم وحكمتهم هو «الوجود المطلق الكلي» أو المشروط بسَلْب الأمور الوجودية. جميع موضوع العلم فموضوع العلم الإلهي الناظر في الوجود ولواحقه هو «الوجود الكلي» المنقسم إلى جوهر وعَرَض وعلة ومعلول، وقديم وحادث وهذا لا وجود له في الخارج، وإنما يوجد في الذهن وهو العلم الأعلى عندهم. فهذا العلم الأعلى مقدر في الذهن. والعليّ الأعلى الموجود في الخارج هو الله الذي لا إله إلا هو، الذي أنزل قوله: ﴿ سَبِّح اسْمَ رَبِّكَ الْأَعْلَى الَّذِي خَلَقَ فَسَوَّى وَالَّذِى قَدَّرَ فَهَدَى وَالَّذِي أَخْرَجَ الْمَرْعَى فَجَعَلَهُ غناء أحوى ﴾ [سورة الأعلى : ١ - ٥]
The foundation of their making their sciences three types. For this reason, when they conceived in their minds what they supposed to exist externally, they made their sciences three types: the lowest in their view is the natural, which does not abstract from matter, neither in the mind nor externally, and it is discourse about body, its properties and divisions. The middle is the mathematical, which abstracts from matter in the mind but not externally, like arithmetic and geometry. For externally there is no quantity except a body, but quantity is abstracted in the soul. The highest in their view is what they call 'science after nature' with respect to demonstrative inference, and 'before nature' with respect to concrete existence, and they may call it 'divine science', and they call it 'first philosophy' and 'highest wisdom', and it is what abstracts from matter in the mind and externally. And if the expert in realities examines their discourse about these types, he does not find with them information existing externally except the section they call 'natural' and what follows from it of the 'mathematical'. As for the mathematical abstracted in the mind, it is judgment about mental quantities having no existence externally. And what they called 'science after nature', which they abstracted from matter in mind and externally, when considered, there is found no knowledge of something existing externally, but rather they conceived estimated matters in their minds having no reality externally. Hence the end of their speculation and the final [point] of their philosophy and wisdom is 'absolute universal existence' or the one conditioned by negation of existential matters. The subject of the science of the divine looking into existence and its concomitants is 'universal existence' divided into substance and accident, cause and effect, eternal and originated; and this has no existence externally, but exists in the mind, and it is their highest science. So this highest science is estimated in the mind. And the highest high existing externally is God, there is no god but He, who sent down His saying: 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Highest, who created and proportioned, and who determined and guided, and who brings forth the pasture, then makes it dry stubble.' [Surah al-A'la, 1-5]
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a critique. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that the philosophers divided their sciences into three types: natural, mathematical, and divine. He explains each: natural science studies bodies and cannot abstract from matter; mathematical science abstracts quantities mentally but not externally; divine science (metaphysics) is said to abstract from matter both mentally and externally. He then argues that upon careful examination, only the natural and some mathematical sciences have external referents. The mathematical abstracted in mind deals with mental quantities, and the divine science is entirely mental constructs—its subject, universal existence, does not exist externally. He contrasts this with the truly Highest, Allah, citing a Quranic verse. The critique is that the philosophers' highest science is not real knowledge but imaginary.

Key Terms
طبيعي (tabi'i): Natural science: what does not abstract from matter in mind or externally, dealing with body and its properties.
رياضي (riyadi): Mathematical science: what abstracts from matter in the mind but not externally, e.g., arithmetic and geometry.
علم ما بعد الطبيعة ('ilm ma ba'd al-tabi'a): Metaphysics: what abstracts from matter in both mind and externally, according to the philosophers. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya critiques this definition, arguing it has no external reality.]
الوجود الكلي (al-wujud al-kulli): Universal existence: the subject of divine science, which exists only in the mind according to Ibn Taymiyya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Natural science only
B) Mathematical science only
C) Both natural and some mathematical
D) Divine science

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the philosophers' metaphysics is a mental construct, not true knowledge of external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the philosophers' classification of sciences according to Ibn Taymiyya's criteria. Which sciences have external reality and which are purely mental? Provide evidence from the passage.
?
Contrast the philosophers' 'highest science' (metaphysics) with the Islamic conception of divine knowledge as revealed in the Quran. How does Ibn Taymiyya use Surah al-A'la to support his point?

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Necessary Existent
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the philosophers' necessary existent is impossible in external reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the philosophers' necessary existent and explain why a being stripped of all positive attributes cannot be real.

Core Idea
The philosophers' necessary existent is a mental fiction, inferior to any positive being.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges the foundational concept of the necessary existent (wajib al-wujud) as understood by the philosophers. He argues that if it is defined purely by negations, any lowly existent with even one positive attribute would be superior. In this lesson, we will dissect his argument and see why the philosophers' concept fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
Ibn Taymiyya lists three ways the philosophers might define the necessary existent: (a) stripped of both positive and negative attributes, (b) stripped of positive but not negative, (c) stripped of both. Find these in the Arabic.
2
Compare to a lowly existent
He then compares any lowly existent to these versions. The lowly existent shares in existence and has at least one positive attribute, while the necessary existent has none. Which is better? He says existence is better than non-existence.
Existence (wujud) is inherently good, so positive attributes add excellence.
3
Draw the conclusion
Since the lowly existent is superior, the philosophers' supposed necessary existent cannot be the most perfect being. In fact, it is an impossible existence, only imagined in the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما واجب الوجود الذي يثبتونه، فإما أن يجعلوه وجوداً مجرداً عن كل قيد يوصف به، فيكون مجرداً عن الأمور الثبوتية كلها، وعن الأمور السلبية. وأما أن يريدوا به أنه لا يوصف بشيء من الأمور الثبوتية مع جواز اتصافه بالأمور السلبية، وأما أن يريدوا به أنه لا يوصف بشيء من الثبوتية ولا السلبية. بل أي موجود حقير فرضته كان خيراً من هذا الذي لا يوصف بشيء ثبوتي، فإنه قد شارك ذلك الموجود الحقير في مسمى الوجود ولم يمتز عنه إلا بوصف عدمي، وذلك الموجود الحقير امتاز عنه بأمر وجودي والوجود خير من العدم، فكان ما امتاز به ذلك الموجود الحقير خيراً مما ميزوا به واجب الوجود بزعمهم. وقد بسط هذا في موضع آخر، وبين أن ما يثبتونه ويجعلونه واجب الوجود هو ممتنع الوجود ولكن يُفرَض في الذهن كما تفرض سائر الممتنعات.
As for the necessary existent they affirm, either they make it an existence stripped of every attribute that describes it, so it is stripped of all positive matters and negative matters. Or they intend by it that it is not described by any positive matter while being possibly described by negative matters, or they intend by it that it is not described by any positive nor negative matter. Rather, any lowly existent you posit is better than this which is not described by any positive attribute, for it shares with that lowly existent the name of existence and is not distinguished from it except by a negative attribute, while that lowly existent is distinguished from it by a positive matter, and existence is better than non-existence, so what distinguishes that lowly existent is better than what they distinguish by it the necessary existent in their claim. And he expounded this in another place, and explained that what they affirm and make the necessary existent is an impossible existence, but it is assumed in the mind as other impossibilities are assumed.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a critique. Ibn Taymiyya examines the philosophers' concept of the necessary existent (wajib al-wujud). He presents three possible interpretations: either it is stripped of both positive and negative attributes, or only of positive, or of both. He then argues that in each case, any lowly existent with some positive attribute is superior, because existence is better than non-existence. The lowly existent shares in existence and has an additional positive attribute, making it better than the philosophers' necessary existent which is defined only by negations. Therefore, their concept is impossible and only a mental construct, like other impossibilities.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud): The necessary existent; a being philosophers claim is necessary, which they strip of all positive attributes, making it a mental construct. [Note: In standard Islamic philosophy, wajib al-wujud is a real entity; Ibn Taymiyya critiques this concept as impossible in external reality.]
ثبوتي (thubuti): Positive attribute; an attribute that affirms existence.
عدمي ('adami): Negative attribute; an attribute of privation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the problem with the philosophers' necessary existent?
A) It has too many positive attributes
B) It is defined entirely by negations, making it inferior to any positive being
C) It exists only in the external world
D) It is identical to the physical world

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya shows that the philosophers' necessary existent, defined by negations, is logically inferior to any actual being, proving it is an impossibility and not the true God.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Vague Universals
Critique of philosophers' empty universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply Ibn Taymiyya's critique of philosophers' universals, and classify any universal into his three types based on its external existence.

Core Idea
A universal without an example is empty knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges philosophers who speak in vague universals without providing concrete examples. When asked for an instance, they retreat to generalities. This reveals that their supposed universal concepts may have no external reality. He then classifies universals into three types: those impossible externally, those merely mental, and those existing but singular.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Locate where Ibn Taymiyya describes how philosophers assert universal matters and pure intellectual universals. Note their refusal to give examples by retreating to 'what is more general' or 'reality as such'.
The philosophers claim to speak about universals, but their inability to give a particular example reveals the emptiness of their claim.
2
Understand the demand for an example
Ibn Taymiyya demands a particular instance (مثال) of their universal. He argues that genuine universal knowledge must have external instances. If they cannot provide an example, their knowledge is merely mental without external reality.
The example is a particular (جزئي) that falls under the universal.
3
Recognize the three types of universals
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes: (1) impossible externally (e.g., partner of God), (2) non-existent but mentally estimated (e.g., goat-deer/chimera), and (3) existent externally but singular (e.g., God, the sun). The philosophers mix these up.
This classification refines the concept of 'universal' and shows that not all universals have multiple external instances.
4
Apply the critique to new claims
When you encounter a universal claim in Islamic sciences, ask: 'What is a concrete example?' If no example can be provided, the claim may be empty or metaphysical speculation.
This tool is especially useful in kalam and usul al-fiqh to test the reality of abstract concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا أنهم كثيراً ما يدّعون في المطالب البرهانية من الأمور العقلية ما عجزهم عن يكونوا قد قدروه في أذهانهم، ويقولون: نحن نتكلم في الأمور الكلية والعقليات الكلية المحضة. وإذا ذُكر لهم شيء قالوا: نتكلم فيما هو أعم من ذلك، وفي «الحقيقة من حيث هي هي»، ونحو هذه العبارات. فيطالبون بتحقيق ما ذكروه في الخارج، ويقال بينوا هذا، أي شيء هو ؟ فهنالك يظهر جهلهم، وأن ما يقولونه هو أمر مقدر في الأذهان، لا حقيقة له في الأعيان. وهذا مثل أن يقال لهم : اذكروا مثال ذلك، والمثال أمر جزئي . فإذا عَجَزوا عن التمثيل وقالوا: نحن نتكلم في الأمور الكلية فاعلم أنهم يتكلمون بلا علم وفيما لا يعلمون أن له معلوماً في الخارج، بل فيما ليس له معلوم في الخارج، وفيما قد يمتنع أن يكون له معلوم في الخارج. وإلا فالعلم بالأمور الموجودة إذا كان كلياً كان له معلومات ثابتة في الخارج . ولفظ «الكلي» يريدون به ما لا يمنع تصوُّرُه من وقوع الشركة فيه. ثم قد يكون معنى لفظ ممتنعاً في الخارج، كشريك الباري. وقد يكون معدوماً وإنما يقدّره الذهن، كما يقدَّر «عنز أيل»، وهذا تمثيل أرسطو . وقد يكون موجوداً في الخارج لكن لا يقبل الشركة، وقد يمكن وقوع الشركة فيه ولم تقع. وهم يمثلون هذا باسم «الإله» و «الشمس»، ويجعلون مسمى هذا كلياً لا يمنع تصوره من وقوع الشركة فيه، وإنما امتنعت الشركة فيه لسبب خارجي فانحصر نوعه في شخصه، لا لمجرّد تصور معناه؛ وهذا مشهور بينهم .
What is intended here is that they often claim in demonstrative inquiries from intellectual matters that they have not been able to [actually] conceive in their minds, and they say: 'We speak about universal matters and pure intellectual universals.' When something is mentioned to them they say: 'We speak about what is more general than that' and about 'reality as such', and similar expressions. So they are required to realize what they mentioned externally, and it is said: 'Make this clear, what is it?' Then their ignorance appears, and that what they say is something estimated in minds, having no reality in external objects. This is like when it is said to them: 'Mention an example of that,' and the example is a particular matter. So if they fail to produce an example and say: 'We speak about universal matters,' then know that they speak without knowledge and about things for which they do not know whether they have a known instance externally, but rather about what has no known instance externally, and about what may be impossible to have a known instance externally. Otherwise, knowledge of existing things, if it is universal, has established known instances externally. And the term 'universal' they intend by it what does not prevent its conception from sharing among multiple instances. Then sometimes a term's meaning is impossible externally, like the partner of the Creator. Sometimes it is non-existent and the mind only estimates it, like the 'goat-deer', and this is Aristotle's example. And sometimes it exists externally but does not admit of sharing, and sometimes sharing in it is possible but has not occurred. They illustrate this with the names 'God' and 'the sun', and they make the named of this universal, not preventing its conception from sharing, but sharing is prevented due to an external cause, so its type is confined to its individual, not merely due to the conception of its meaning; and this is well-known among them.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a critique. Ibn Taymiyya exposes the philosophers' practice of making claims about universals without providing concrete examples. When challenged, they retreat to broader generalities, revealing their ignorance. He then discusses the term 'universal' (kulli) as they define it: something that does not prevent mental sharing. He distinguishes three types of universals: (1) impossible in external reality, like a partner of God; (2) nonexistent but mentally conceived, like a goat-deer (a chimera); (3) existent externally but not actually shared due to external circumstances, like God and the sun. Ibn Taymiyya argues that genuine universal knowledge must have external instances; without examples, their claims are empty.

الكلي
المثال
جزئي
تمثيل
شريك الباري
عنز أيل

A) Partner of God
B) Goat-deer (chimera)
C) The sun
D) Animal

Key Takeaway
Universal claims without concrete examples are epistemically empty; true universal knowledge must have verifiable external instances.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Sun and God as Particulars
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' claim that 'sun' and 'God' are universal terms, arguing they are used as particulars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that 'sun' and 'God' are particulars and identify the linguistic and epistemological grounds of his critique.

Core Idea
Common usage, not logical abstraction, determines whether a term is universal or particular.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya challenges the logicians' claim that terms like 'sun' and 'God' are universals. He uses Quranic evidence and everyday language to show that these terms refer to specific entities, not abstract classes. This critique is part of his broader attack on the epistemological pretensions of Greek logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Understand that Ibn Taymiyya is critiquing the standard logical position that words like 'sun' and 'God' are universals (kulliyat) that apply to many individuals.
2
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's key distinction
Notice how he distinguishes between a generic noun (ism al-jins) and actual usage. He argues that in Arabic, the definite article in 'al-shams' indicates a specific entity ('the covenant' definition), not the genus.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the verse from Surah al-Anbiya (21:33). Ibn Taymiyya uses it to show that 'sun' and 'moon' refer to particular celestial bodies created by Allah, not to a universal class.
4
Understand the consequence
If 'sun' and 'God' are particulars, then the universal-particular distinction is not as clear-cut as logicians claim. Ibn Taymiyya generalizes this to many proper nouns in religious language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما يصح هذا إذا كان لفظ «الإله» ولفظ «الشمس» اسم جنس بحيث لا يُقصد به الشمس المعيّنة ولا الإله المعين المعروف فإن الكلي عندهم مثل «اسم الجنس» في اصطلاح النحاة، وهو ما عُلّق على الشيء وعلى كل ما أشبهه. والناس لا يقصدون بلفظ «الشمس» إلا الشمس المعيّنة، واللام فيها لتعريف العهد، لا للجنس، كما قال تعالى : ﴿ وَهُوَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ الَّيْلَ وَالنَّهَارَ وَالشَّمْسَ وَالْقَمَرَ كُلٌّ فِي فَلَكٍ يَسْبَحُونَ ﴾ [سورة الأنبياء : ٣٣]. فمسمى «الشمس» و «القمر» هنا جزئي لا كلي بخلاف لفظ «الكوكب» والنجم ونحو ذلك، فإنه كلي وكذلك اسم «الإله» عند المسلمين إنما يريدون به إلههم، وهو «الله» لا إله إلا هو
وعلى هذا فليس هذا ولا هذا كليّاً مشتركاً، بل نفس تصور معناه يمنع من وقوع الشركة فيه . فهو معيَّن مختص، وهو الذي يسمونه «الجزئي»، ليس مطلقاً مشتركاً، وهو الذي يسمونه «الكلي».
حيرة الخسرو وكان الخسرو شاهي من أعيانهم وأعيان أصحاب الرازي. وكان يقول: «ما عثرنا إلا على هذه الكليات» وكان قد وقع في حَيْرة وشكّ ، حتى كان يقول : والله ! ما أدري ما أعتقد والله ما أدري ما أعتقد والله ! ما أدري ما أعتقد.
This is only correct if the word 'God' and the word 'sun' are generic nouns, such that the specific sun is not intended nor the specific known God. For the universal according to them is like the generic noun in the terminology of grammarians, which is attached to a thing and to everything similar to it. But people do not intend by the word 'sun' except the specific sun, and the lam in it is for definition of the covenant, not for the genus, as Allah said: 'And He it is who created the night and the day and the sun and the moon, all in an orbit floating.' So the denotation of 'sun' and 'moon' here is particular, not universal, unlike the word 'planet' and 'star' and the like, for it is universal. Likewise, the name 'God' among Muslims only means their God, who is Allah, there is no god but Him. On this basis, neither this nor that is a shared universal; rather, the very conception of its meaning prevents sharing in it. So it is specific and particular, which they call 'the particular,' not absolute and shared, which they call 'the universal.' The confusion of al-Khusrawshahi: He was one of their prominent figures and prominent companions of al-Razi. He used to say: 'We have only stumbled upon these universals.' And he fell into confusion and doubt, until he would say: 'By Allah, I do not know what I believe! By Allah, I do not know what I believe! By Allah, I do not know what I believe!'
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument from Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' notion that terms like 'sun' and 'God' are universals. He begins by stating that the logicians' concept only holds if these words are generic nouns, but in common usage people refer to the specific sun and their specific God. He provides Quranic evidence from Surah al-Anbiya (21:33) where 'sun' and 'moon' are mentioned as particular objects. He distinguishes between words like 'planet' which are truly universal and words like 'sun' which are used as proper names. He then generalizes: neither 'sun' nor 'God' are shared universals because their meanings are inherently specific. Finally, he relates an anecdote about al-Khusrawshahi, a follower of al-Razi, who became so confused about universals that he repeatedly exclaimed his uncertainty about what to believe. This anecdote underscores the practical confusion that results from the logicians' doctrine.

كلي
جزئي
اسم الجنس

A) Because it is a proper noun

D) Because it is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
Language used in common discourse often treats proper nouns as particulars, undermining the logicians' purely conceptual universals.

Refuting the Myth of Absolute Scepticism
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the existence of Sufasta and total denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that total denial of all truths is impossible for any living being.

Core Idea
No living being can deny all realities; even animals have basic awareness.

Ibn Taymiyya challenges a widespread claim among theologians that there existed a group called al-Sufasta who denied all truths. He argues that such total denial is impossible because basic survival requires recognizing sensory and practical realities. This lesson unpacks his argument, which relies on natural human experience and social nature.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of refutation
Find the claim that Ibn Taymiyya is refuting: that there is a man called Sufasta and his followers who deny all realities and sciences.
This is a common polemical trope in kalām literature.
2
Extract the first argument: impossibility of life without basic awareness
Ibn Taymiyya says it is inconceivable for any human or animal to live while denying all realities. He lists basic necessities like hunger, thirst, and distinguishing between things.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if total denial were true, life itself would be impossible.
3
Analyze the social nature argument
Since humans are social by nature (madaniyyun bi al-tabʿ), they must recognize other people and communicate. This implies shared knowledge of realities.
Ibn Taymiyya uses a premise from political philosophy: man is a social animal.
4
Assess the strength of the argument
Consider whether Ibn Taymiyya's argument successfully refutes the claim. Does it rely on empirical impossibility or conceptual necessity?
He treats the claim as self-evidently false, not requiring formal proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنكار العقل وأما ما يذكره طائفة من أهل الكلام وناقلي المقالات أن في الناس رجل يقال له : بأن في الناس سوفسطا»، وأنه وأصحابه ينكرون جميع الحقائق والعلوم، فهذا باطل لا حقيقة له. ولا يتصور أن يعيش أحد من بني آدم بل ولا من البهائم مع جحد جميع الحقائق والشعور بها . فإن الإنسان مدني بالطبع، فلا بد أن يعرف بعض الناس بعضاً. ويعرف الإنسان جوعه وشبعه، وعطشه ورِيَّه، ولذته وألمه، ويميز بين ما يأكله وما لا يأكله، وما يلبسه وما لا يلبسه، وبين مسكنه ومسكن جاره وبين الليل والنهار وغير ذلك من الأمور التي هي ضرورية في الحياة . إبطال النقل وكذلك ما يذكرونه أن في السُّمَنِيَّة (۱) قوم ينكرون من العلوم ما سوی حتى ينكروا «المتواترات» غَلَط على القوم . فإنهم أنكروا وجود ما لا يمكن الإحساس به ، من العلوم ما لم ينكروا وجودَ ما لا يُحِسُّونه هم. وقد ذكر الإمام أحمد مناظرتهم [٣١٦] للجهم بن صفوان (۱) ، وهي تقتضي ذلك. وإلا فهؤلاء من عقلاء/ الهند وحُكمائهم، /٣٣٠ وإن كانوا مشركين يعبدون الأصنام. فلا يُتصوّر أن أحدهم ينكر ما كان قبل مولده، فلا ينكر وجود البلاد والأنهار والجبال والدور التي لم يرها؛ ولا ينكر وجود كل إنسان أو بهيمة لم يرها . فإن هذا ليس عليه أحد من بني آدم، بل بنو آدم كلُّهم متفقون على أن ما شاهدوه عَلِموه بالمشاهدة، وما غاب عنهم علموه بالأخبار. فلا يتصور أن كل طائفة من الطوائف تتفق على جحد جنس الأخبار . طريقة قد تستعمل لإزالة السفسطة ولكن قد تعرض السَّفْسَطة لبعض الطوائف ولبعض الأشخاص في بعض المعارف، فإن أمراض القلوب كأمراض الأجسام. فكما أنها ليس في الوجود أمة ولا شخص يمرض بكل مرض، فليس فيهم من هو جاهل بكل شيء، وفاسد الاعتقاد في كل شيء. وقد ذكرت طريقة تستعمل لإزالة السفسطة.
Denial of reason: As for what a group of theologians and transmitters of doctrines mention, that among people there is a man called 'Sufasta' and that he and his followers deny all realities and sciences—this is false and has no reality. It is inconceivable that any human being, or even animal, could live while denying all realities and awareness of them. For man is social by nature, so he must know some people. He knows his hunger and satiety, thirst and quenching, pleasure and pain, and distinguishes between what he eats and what he does not, what he wears and what he does not, between his dwelling and his neighbor's dwelling, between night and day, and other matters that are necessary for life. Invalidation of transmission: Similarly, what they mention that among the Sumaniyya there are people who deny all sciences except the senses, to the point that they deny 'mutawatir' reports—this is a mistake about them. For they denied the existence of what cannot be sensed, not that they denied the existence of what they themselves do not sense. Imam Ahmad has mentioned their debate with Jahm ibn Safwan, and it implies that. Otherwise, they are among the wise men of India and their philosophers, even if they are polytheists worshipping idols. So it is inconceivable that any of them denies what existed before his birth, so he does not deny the existence of countries, rivers, mountains, and houses that he has not seen; nor does he deny the existence of every human or animal he has not seen. For no human being does this; rather, all humans agree that what they witness they know by witnessing, and what is absent from them they know by reports. So it is inconceivable that any group of groups agrees to deny the entire category of reports. A method that may be used to remove sophistry: But sophistry may occur in some groups and some individuals regarding some cognitions. For diseases of the hearts are like diseases of bodies. Just as there is no nation or person that is sick with every disease, so there is none who is ignorant of everything and corrupt in belief about everything. I have mentioned a method that may be used to remove sophistry.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that there exists a person or group (called 'Sufasta') who deny all truths and sciences. Ibn Taymiyya argues that such total denial is impossible for any living being, because basic self-preservation requires recognizing sensory and practical matters like hunger, thirst, and distinguishing things. He then addresses the specific case of al-Sumaniyya, who are accused of denying all knowledge except the senses. He corrects this: they only deny what cannot be sensed, not all knowledge. Even they accept testimonial knowledge about things they have not seen, as do all humans. Finally, he notes that sophistry is a partial disease of the heart, not total, and that he has described a method to cure it. This is a critical refutation of exaggerated claims about scepticism.

Key Terms
السوفسطا
المتواترات
أمراض القلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why is the claim about Sufasta false?
A) Because Sufasta is a mythical figure not mentioned in any reliable source.
B) Because no living being can deny all realities and survive, as basic needs require awareness.
C) Because the logicians proved that total denial is self-refuting using syllogisms.
D) Because al-Sumaniyya admitted that they accept sensory knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Total denial of all truths is impossible because basic life and social interaction require minimal awareness and recognition of realities.
