Correcting the Position of al-Sumaniyya
Ibn Taymiyya corrects the misrepresentation of al-Sumaniyya and discusses the partial nature of sophistry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-Sumaniyya's actual position (denial of non-sensible entities) and the misattribution of denying all transmitted knowledge.

Core Idea
Al-Sumaniyya only deny what cannot be sensed, not all reports—a common misrepresentation.

Ibn Taymiyya corrects a common error about al-Sumaniyya, often portrayed as denying all knowledge except the senses. He explains that they only reject knowledge of entities that cannot be sensed, but they accept testimonial reports about the unseen. This lesson also discusses the partial nature of sophistry as a disease of the heart.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misrepresentation
Find the claim that al-Sumaniyya deny all sciences except the senses, even mutawatir reports. Ibn Taymiyya calls this a mistake (ghalat).
This is a critical refinement: he is correcting an inaccurate report about their views.
2
Extract the correction
Ibn Taymiyya says they only deny the existence of what cannot be sensed (ma la yumkin al-ihsas bihi), not that they deny what they themselves do not sense. In other words, they are empiricists, not total relativists.
They accept testimonial knowledge about things they have not personally seen.
3
Consider the evidence from Imam Ahmad
He references Imam Ahmad's account of their debate with Jahm ibn Safwan, which supports this interpretation.
This shows he relies on historical precedent, not just argument.
4
Relate to the metaphor of heart diseases
Ibn Taymiyya compares sophistry (safsata) to diseases of the body: just as no one is sick with every disease, no one is ignorant of everything. Sophistry is partial, not total.
This reinforces that absolute denial is impossible, and even those affected by sophistry have some correct knowledge.

إنكار العقل وأما ما يذكره طائفة من أهل الكلام وناقلي المقالات أن في الناس رجل يقال له : بأن في الناس سوفسطا»، وأنه وأصحابه ينكرون جميع الحقائق والعلوم، فهذا باطل لا حقيقة له. ولا يتصور أن يعيش أحد من بني آدم بل ولا من البهائم مع جحد جميع الحقائق والشعور بها . فإن الإنسان مدني بالطبع، فلا بد أن يعرف بعض الناس بعضاً. ويعرف الإنسان جوعه وشبعه، وعطشه ورِيَّه، ولذته وألمه، ويميز بين ما يأكله وما لا يأكله، وما يلبسه وما لا يلبسه، وبين مسكنه ومسكن جاره وبين الليل والنهار وغير ذلك من الأمور التي هي ضرورية في الحياة . إبطال النقل وكذلك ما يذكرونه أن في السُّمَنِيَّة (۱) قوم ينكرون من العلوم ما سوی حتى ينكروا «المتواترات» غَلَط على القوم . فإنهم أنكروا وجود ما لا يمكن الإحساس به ، من العلوم ما لم ينكروا وجودَ ما لا يُحِسُّونه هم. وقد ذكر الإمام أحمد مناظرتهم [٣١٦] للجهم بن صفوان (۱) ، وهي تقتضي ذلك. وإلا فهؤلاء من عقلاء/ الهند وحُكمائهم، /٣٣٠ وإن كانوا مشركين يعبدون الأصنام. فلا يُتصوّر أن أحدهم ينكر ما كان قبل مولده، فلا ينكر وجود البلاد والأنهار والجبال والدور التي لم يرها؛ ولا ينكر وجود كل إنسان أو بهيمة لم يرها . فإن هذا ليس عليه أحد من بني آدم، بل بنو آدم كلُّهم متفقون على أن ما شاهدوه عَلِموه بالمشاهدة، وما غاب عنهم علموه بالأخبار. فلا يتصور أن كل طائفة من الطوائف تتفق على جحد جنس الأخبار . طريقة قد تستعمل لإزالة السفسطة ولكن قد تعرض السَّفْسَطة لبعض الطوائف ولبعض الأشخاص في بعض المعارف، فإن أمراض القلوب كأمراض الأجسام. فكما أنها ليس في الوجود أمة ولا شخص يمرض بكل مرض، فليس فيهم من هو جاهل بكل شيء، وفاسد الاعتقاد في كل شيء. وقد ذكرت طريقة تستعمل لإزالة السفسطة.
Denial of reason: As for what a group of theologians and transmitters of doctrines mention, that among people there is a man called 'Sufasta' and that he and his followers deny all realities and sciences—this is false and has no reality. It is inconceivable that any human being, or even animal, could live while denying all realities and awareness of them. For man is social by nature, so he must know some people. He knows his hunger and satiety, thirst and quenching, pleasure and pain, and distinguishes between what he eats and what he does not, what he wears and what he does not, between his dwelling and his neighbor's dwelling, between night and day, and other matters that are necessary for life. Invalidation of transmission: Similarly, what they mention that among the Sumaniyya there are people who deny all sciences except the senses, to the point that they deny 'mutawatir' reports—this is a mistake about them. For they denied the existence of what cannot be sensed, not that they denied the existence of what they themselves do not sense. Imam Ahmad has mentioned their debate with Jahm ibn Safwan, and it implies that. Otherwise, they are among the wise men of India and their philosophers, even if they are polytheists worshipping idols. So it is inconceivable that any of them denies what existed before his birth, so he does not deny the existence of countries, rivers, mountains, and houses that he has not seen; nor does he deny the existence of every human or animal he has not seen. For no human being does this; rather, all humans agree that what they witness they know by witnessing, and what is absent from them they know by reports. So it is inconceivable that any group of groups agrees to deny the entire category of reports. A method that may be used to remove sophistry: But sophistry may occur in some groups and some individuals regarding some cognitions. For diseases of the hearts are like diseases of bodies. Just as there is no nation or person that is sick with every disease, so there is none who is ignorant of everything and corrupt in belief about everything. I have mentioned a method that may be used to remove sophistry.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that there exists a person or group (called 'Sufasta') who deny all truths and sciences. Ibn Taymiyya argues that such total denial is impossible for any living being, because basic self-preservation requires recognizing sensory and practical matters like hunger, thirst, and distinguishing things. He then addresses the specific case of al-Sumaniyya, who are accused of denying all knowledge except the senses. He corrects this: they only deny what cannot be sensed, not all knowledge. Even they accept testimonial knowledge about things they have not seen, as do all humans. Finally, he notes that sophistry is a partial disease of the heart, not total, and that he has described a method to cure it. This is a critical refutation of exaggerated claims about scepticism.

Key Terms
السمنية
المتواترات
أمراض القلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the accurate position of al-Sumaniyya?
A) They deny all knowledge, including sensory knowledge.
B) They deny the existence of what cannot be sensed, but accept testimonial reports about the unseen.
C) They accept all transmitted reports including mutawatir.
D) They are polytheists who reject all laws.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sumaniyya's denial is limited to non-sensible entities, not all knowledge; sophistry is always partial, never total.

Identifying the Peripatetic School: Origins and Internal Critiques
Ibn Taymiyya identifies the Peripatetic school and notes variations among its followers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Peripatetic school as defined by Ibn Taymiyya and describe how later scholars critically engaged with it.

Core Idea
The Peripatetic school was not a monolith; even its followers disagreed and critiqued each other.

Ibn Taymiyya begins his critique of Aristotelian logic by clearly identifying his target: the Peripatetic school, the followers of Aristotle. But he immediately complicates the picture by showing that this school was not a unified bloc. He lists major figures who transmitted or critiqued Peripatetic doctrines, including al-Ghazali, al-Razi, al-Amidi, Ibn Sina, Abu al-Barakat, and al-Suhrawardi. Understanding this diversity is crucial for grasping Ibn Taymiyya's strategy: he is not attacking a straw man but engaging with a living tradition that had internal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target school
Locate the term 'المشائين' (Peripatetics) in the passage. Note that they are defined as followers of Aristotle, the author of logic. This is the school Ibn Taymiyya is refuting.
The term 'أتباع أرسطو' is used synonymously.
2
List scholars and their engagement
Find each scholar mentioned (al-Ghazali, al-Razi, al-Amidi, Abu al-Barakat, Ibn Sina, al-Suhrawardi) and note how they engaged with Peripatetic thought: presentation, refutation, objection, independent evaluation, or revision.
Pay attention to phrases like 'يعترضون عليهم' (object to them) and 'لم يقلدهم' (did not imitate).
3
Recognize internal disagreements
Note that even Ibn Sina, a leading Peripatetic, disagreed with earlier Peripatetics. The passage mentions his book 'al-Shifa' and his separate work 'al-Hikma al-Mashriqiyya' where he placed the truth as he saw it. This shows internal revision.
Al-Suhrawardi and al-Razi also produced their own independent works.
4
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's purpose
Reflect on why Ibn Taymiyya lists these disagreements before his critique. It shows that the Peripatetic tradition is not monolithic, and that his critique fits within an ongoing scholarly conversation where critical evaluation is already present.
This strengthens his argument: he is not attacking a straw man.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه الثاني : أنصار مذهب المشائين أتباع أرسطو المشائين أتباع أرسطو صاحب المنطق. وهو الذي يذكره الغزالي في كتاب «مقاصد الفلاسفة»، وعليه رَدَّ في «التهافت». وهو الذي يذكره الرازي في «الملخص» و «المباحث المشرقية»، ويذكره الآمدي في «دقائق الحقائق» و «رموز الكنوز» وغير ذلك. وعلى طريقهم مشى أبو البركات صاحبُ «المعتبر»، لكن لم يقلدهم تقليد غيره، بل اعتبر ما ذكروه بحسب نظره وعقله. وكذلك الرازي والآمدي يعترضان عليهم في كثير مما يذكرونه بحسب ما يسنح لهم. وابن سينا أيضاً قد يخالف الأولين في بعض ما ذكروه. ولهذا ذكر في كتابه المسمى بـ «الشفاء» أن الحق الذي ثبت عنده ذكره في «الحكمة المشرقية». والسهروردي ذكر ما ثبت عنده في «حكمة الإشراق». والرازي في «المباحث المشرقية».
The Fourth Station - The Second Aspect: Supporters of the doctrine of the Peripatetics, followers of Aristotle. The Peripatetics are the followers of Aristotle, the author of logic. It is he whom al-Ghazali mentions in his book 'Maqasid al-Falasifa', and upon which he refuted in 'al-Tahafut'. And it is he whom al-Razi mentions in 'al-Mulakhkhas' and 'al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya', and al-Amidi mentions in 'Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq' and 'Rumuz al-Kunuz' and others. And upon their path walked Abu al-Barakat, author of 'al-Mu'tabar', but he did not blindly imitate them as others did; rather, he considered what they mentioned according to his own observation and intellect. Likewise, al-Razi and al-Amidi object to them in much of what they mention according to what occurs to them. And Ibn Sina also sometimes disagreed with the earlier ones in some of what they mentioned. For this reason, he stated in his book called 'al-Shifa' that the truth confirmed with him he mentioned in 'al-Hikma al-Mashriqiyya'. And al-Suhrawardi mentioned what was confirmed with him in 'Hikmat al-Ishraq'. And al-Razi in 'al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya'.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a critical identification of the Peripatetic school by Ibn Taymiyya. He defines the Peripatetics as followers of Aristotle, the father of logic. Ibn Taymiyya then notes that several prominent scholars engaged with their works: al-Ghazali presented their doctrines in 'Maqasid al-Falasifa' and refuted them in 'Tahafut al-Falasifa'; al-Razi discussed them in 'al-Mulakhkhas' and 'al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya'; al-Amidi in 'Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq' and 'Rumuz al-Kunuz'. The passage then highlights that not all followers of the Peripatetic method were uncritical. Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, author of 'al-Mu'tabar', followed their path but did not blindly imitate; rather, he evaluated their claims based on his own reason. Similarly, al-Razi and al-Amidi raised objections to many of their statements according to what occurred to them. Ibn Taymiyya also points out that even Ibn Sina, a leading Peripatetic, sometimes disagreed with earlier Peripatetics, as evidenced by his statement in 'al-Shifa' that the truth he confirmed he placed in 'al-Hikma al-Mashriqiyya'. Al-Suhrawardi and al-Razi also wrote their own works ('Hikmat al-Ishraq' and 'al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya') presenting what they held as true. Thus, the passage emphasizes the diversity within the Peripatetic tradition and the critical engagement of later scholars.

Key Terms
المشائين
أتباع أرسطو
المنطق

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's identification of the Peripatetic school includes a careful mapping of its internal diversity, which strengthens his critique by showing the tradition's own critical spirit.

The Cause of Need for the Creator: Imkan vs Huduth
Ibn Taymiyya discusses the debate over whether possibility or temporal origination is the cause of need for a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two competing positions on the cause of the world's need for a creator and explain Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the 'imkan alone' position.

Core Idea
The debate over possibility vs. temporal origination is a key fault line between philosophers and theologians.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya tackles a fundamental theological question: what exactly makes the world need a creator? Is it the mere possibility of its existence, or the fact that it came into being after non-existence? Later philosophers, including Ibn Sina, argued that possibility alone suffices, which would allow for an eternal yet contingent world. Ibn Taymiyya rejects this, citing a broad consensus that whatever is possible must also be temporally originated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
Read the opening lines: 'الكلام على علة الافتقار إلى الصانع'. What is the cause (علة) that makes the world depend on a creator? Note the two options presented: الإمكان (possibility) and الحدوث (temporal origination).
2
Locate the two competing positions
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states the first position: that مجرد الإمكان بدون الحدوث (mere possibility without temporal origination) necessitates the need for a creator. Then identify the second view, implied as the correct one: that possibility alone is insufficient; temporal origination is also required.
3
Identify the proponents of each view
According to the passage, who holds the first position? Ibn Taymiyya names المتفلسفة المتأخرين (the later philosophers) and من وافقهم كالرازي (those who agreed with them, like al-Razi). Who holds the second? He says جماهير من الأولين والآخرين (the majority of earlier and later thinkers), including Aristotle, ancient philosophers, and Ibn Rushd.
4
Trace Ibn Taymiyya's argumentative strategy
Ibn Taymiyya does not give a detailed logical proof here; instead, he appeals to consensus (اتفق) and authority (even Aristotle and ancient philosophers agree that whatever is possible is temporally originated). This is a critical tactic to undermine the novelty and credibility of Ibn Sina's view.
5
Evaluate the critique
Consider: Is this a purely logical refutation or an appeal to authority? Ibn Taymiyya uses the agreement of philosophers he normally criticizes (like Ibn Rushd) to strengthen his case. Reflect on how this aligns with his broader approach in 'Radd al-Mantiqiyyin'.
This passage is part of a larger critical refinement of the philosophers' arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على علة الافتقار إلى الصانع وقد بسط الكلام على هذا في مواضع مثل ما ذكرناه من طرق إثبات العلم أو الحدوث بالصانع، والطرق التي سلكها عامة النظار في هذا المطلوب، وفي الكلام على أو مجموعهما «المحصل، وغير ذلك. فإن المتأخرين من النظار تكلموا في «علة الافتقار إلى المؤثر - وإن شئت قلت : إلى الصانع - هل هو الإمكان»، أو «الحدوث»، أو مجموعهما. فالأول قولُ المتفلسفة المتأخرين ومن وافقهم كالرازي. ومقصودهم بذلك أن مجرد الإمكان» بدون «الحدوث يوجب الافتقار إلى الصانع، فيمكن كونُ «الممكن» قديماً، لا محدثاً مع كونه مفتقراً إلى المؤثر. وهذا القول مما اتفق جماهير من الأولين والآخرين على فساده، حتى أرسطو وقدماء الفلاسفة ومن اتبعه من متأخريهم، كابن رشد الحفيد وغيره كلهم يقولون إن ما أمكن وجوده وأمكن عدمه لا يكون إلا محدثاً». وإنما قال هذا القول ابن سينا وأمثاله واتبعهم الرازي وأمثاله.
Discourse on the cause of the need for the Creator. And we have elaborated on this in other places, such as what we mentioned of the methods of proving knowledge or temporal origination by the Creator, and the methods that the generality of the theorists have followed in this aim, and in the discourse on 'al-Muhassal,' etc. For the later theorists have discussed the 'cause of the need for the effective agent'—and if you wish, say 'for the Creator'—whether it is 'possibility' (imkan) or 'temporal origination' (huduth) or both together. The first is the statement of the later philosophers and those who agree with them, like al-Razi. Their intention is that mere possibility without temporal origination necessitates the need for the Creator, so it is possible for something possible to be eternal, not temporally originated, while still needing the effective agent. And this statement is agreed upon by the majority of the earlier and later thinkers as being corrupt, even Aristotle and the ancient philosophers and those who followed them among the later ones, like Ibn Rushd the grandson and others, all of them say that whatever it is possible to exist and possible not to exist cannot be except as temporally originated. Only Ibn Sina and his likes said this statement, and al-Razi and his likes followed them.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a critical overview of the theological debate regarding the cause of the world's need for a creator. Ibn Taymiyya sets up the question: what is the 'illa (cause) that necessitates the need for an effective agent (mu'aththir) or creator (sani')? Is it the possibility (imkan) of the world, its temporal origination (huduth), or both? He identifies the first position—that mere possibility is sufficient—as the view of the later philosophers (like Ibn Sina) and those who followed them, such as Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. Their reasoning is that if mere possibility is the cause, then a possible being could be eternal (qadim) while still needing a creator. Ibn Taymiyya then asserts that the great majority of thinkers, from Aristotle to the ancients and even Ibn Rushd, reject this, holding that whatever is possible (i.e., could exist or not exist) must be temporally originated (hadith). Thus, according to the consensus of the 'majority of earlier and later thinkers,' the claim that mere possibility suffices is corrupt (fasid). Only Ibn Sina and his followers held this view. This passage serves to refute the philosophical position that the world could be eternal yet contingent, which is a key point of disagreement between Islamic theologians and philosophers.

Key Terms
علة الافتقار
الإمكان
الحدوث
المتفلسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, which group holds that mere possibility (imkan) is the cause of the need for a creator?
A) The early philosophers like Aristotle
B) The later philosophers like Ibn Sina and al-Razi
C) The majority of Muslim theologians
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the philosophical claim that mere possibility necessitates a creator, asserting that possibility must be accompanied by temporal origination, a view he supports by citing a broad consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Write a brief summary (2-3 sentences) explaining the two positions on the cause of the need for a creator, and identify which one Ibn Taymiyya rejects.
?
Task 2: Find another passage in the 'Radd al-Mantiqiyyin' where Ibn Taymiyya uses a similar consensus-based argument to refute a philosophical claim. Compare his strategy with the one in this passage.

Ibn Taymiyya on the Cause of Dependence on the Creator
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of three views on the cause of dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's position on the cause of dependence from the three views he critiques and articulate why he considers them invalid.

Core Idea
Dependence on Allah is an inherent attribute of creatures, not caused by possibility or origination.

Ibn Taymiyya confronts a key metaphysical question: what makes creatures dependent on the Creator? He examines three answers from philosophers and theologians, and then presents his own radical alternative. This lesson will walk you through his critique and his positive doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
Find the three positions that later theorists took on the 'cause of dependence' (illa al-iftiqar). They are: (1) mere possibility (imkan), (2) temporal origination (huduth), (3) both. Note who held each view.
2
Understand the rejection of each view
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the first view because it implies an eternal possible dependent on a cause, which even Aristotle and ancient philosophers denied. The second view (only huduth) is also corrupt. The third view (both) is only acceptable if understood as indicators, not causes.
Notice that he uses the agreement of earlier thinkers and logical contradictions to refute.
3
Grasp his alternative
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that poverty and need are essential to creatures (lazim li-dhatihi). They are not caused by any quality like imkan or huduth. These qualities are merely signs (adilla) that reveal the inherent dependence.
Key phrase: 'فالفقر لازم لذاته' – poverty is necessary to its essence.
4
Connect to the divine name al-Samad
Ibn Taymiyya invokes the Qur'anic name al-Samad (the Independent, the Needed) to illustrate that all creatures are permanently dependent on Allah, while He is independent. This supports the idea that dependence is an unchanging property.
Just as Allah's richness is self-subsistent, creation's poverty is self-subsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بسط الكلام على هذا في مواضع مثل ما ذكرناه من طرق إثبات العلم أو الحدوث بالصانع، والطرق التي سلكها عامة النظار في هذا المطلوب، وفي الكلام على المحصل، وغير ذلك. فإن المتأخرين من النظار تكلموا في «علة الافتقار إلى المؤثر - وإن شئت قلت : إلى الصانع - هل هو الإمكان»، أو «الحدوث»، أو مجموعهما. فالأول قولُ المتفلسفة المتأخرين ومن وافقهم كالرازي. ومقصودهم بذلك أن مجرد الإمكان بدون الحدوث يوجب الافتقار إلى الصانع، فيمكن كون الممكن قديماً، لا محدثاً مع كونه مفتقراً إلى المؤثر. وهذا القول مما اتفق جماهير من الأولين والآخرين على فساده، حتى أرسطو وقدماء الفلاسفة ومن اتبعه من متأخريهم، كابن رشد الحفيد وغيره كلهم يقولون إن ما أمكن وجوده وأمكن عدمه لا يكون إلا محدثاً. وإنما قال هذا القول ابن سينا وأمثاله واتبعهم الرازي وأمثاله. وهؤلاء يجعلون الشيء الممكن مفتقراً إلى الفاعل في حال بقائه فقط، فإنه لم يكن له حال حدوث . ولهذا لما جعلوا مثل هذا ممكناً اضطرب كلامهم في «الممكن»، وورد عليهم إشكالات لا جواب لهم عنها كما ذكر في كتبه كلها الكبار والصغار، كـ «الأربعين»، و « نهاية العقول»، و «المطالب العالية»، و «المحصل»، وغيرها. وقد بسطناه في غير هذا الموضع . والقول الثاني : إن علة الافتقار مجرد «الحدوث، وإن المحدث يفتقر إلى الفاعل حال حدوثه، لا حال بقائه. وهذا قول طائفة من أهل الكلام - المعتزلة وغيرهم بالحدوث فقط. وهذا أيضاً قول فاسد . والقول الثالث: إن علة الافتقار هي الإمكان والحدوث. ولم يجعل أحدهما شرطاً في الآخر، وقد يجعل أحد الشطرين. وقد بينا في غير هذا الموضع أن كل واحد من «الحدوث» و «الإمكان» دليل على الافتقار إلى الصانع وإن كانا متلازمين . فإذا علمنا أن هذا محدث علمنا أنه مفتقر إلى من يُحدِثه، وإذا علمنا أن هذا ممكن وجوده وممكن عدمه علمنا أنه لا يرجح وجوده على عدمه إلا بفاعل يجعله موجوداً. وكونه مفتقراً إلى الفاعل هو من لوازم حقيقته، لا يحتاج أن يعلل بعلة جعلته مفتقراً، بل الفقر لازم لذاته. فكل ما سوى الله فقير إليه دائماً، لا يستغني عنه طرفة عين. وهذا من معاني اسمه الصمد. فالصمد الذي يحتاج إليه كل شيء وهو مستغن عن كل شيء. وكما أن غنى الرب ثبت له لنفسه لا لعلة، ففقر المخلوقات وحاجتها إليه ثبت لذواتها، لا لعلة جعلتها مفتقرة إليه . فمن قال : علة الافتقار إلى الفاعل هي الحدوث، أو الإمكان، أو مجموعهما، إن أراد أن هذه المعاني جعلت الذات فقيرة لم يصح شيء من ذلك. وإن أراد أن هذه المعاني يُعلم بها فقر الذات فهو حق . فكل منهما مستلزم لفقر الذات، وهي مفتقرة إليه حال حدوثها وحال بقائها لا يمكن استغناؤها عنه لا في هذه الحال ولا في هذه الحال .
And we have elaborated on this in other places such as the methods we mentioned for proving the Maker through knowledge or temporal origination, and the methods followed by most theorists in this inquiry, and in the discussion on al-Muhassal, and others. For the later theorists discussed the 'cause of dependence on the efficient cause' – if you wish, say: on the Maker – is it possibility, or temporal origination, or both? The first is the view of the later philosophers and those who agree with them like al-Razi. Their aim is that mere possibility without temporal origination necessitates dependence on the Maker, so it is possible for the possible to be eternal, not originated, while still being dependent on the efficient cause. This view is unanimously rejected by the majority of earlier and later thinkers, even Aristotle and the ancient philosophers and those who followed them among the later ones, like Ibn Rushd the grandson and others; they all say that what is possible of existence and possible of non-existence cannot but be originated. Only Ibn Sina and his ilk said this, and al-Razi and his ilk followed them. And these people make the possible thing dependent on the agent only in the state of its persistence, for it has no state of origination. Hence when they made something like this possible, their speech about 'the possible' became confused, and objections arose for which they have no answer, as mentioned in all his books, large and small, like al-Arba'in, Nihayat al-Uqul, al-Matalib al-Aliya, al-Muhassal, and others. And we have elaborated on this elsewhere. The second view: that the cause of dependence is merely temporal origination, and that the originated thing depends on the agent at the moment of its origination, not at the moment of its persistence. This is the view of a group of theologians – the Mu'tazila and others – based solely on temporal origination. This is also a corrupt view. The third view: that the cause of dependence is possibility and temporal origination together. Some do not make one a condition for the other, and some make one of the two parts. We have shown elsewhere that each of 'temporal origination' and 'possibility' is evidence for dependence on the Maker, even if they are concomitant. So if we know that something is originated, we know that it depends on the One who originated it; and if we know that something is possible of existence and possible of non-existence, we know that its existence is not preferred over its non-existence except by a Maker who brings it into existence. And its being dependent on the agent is among the necessities of its essence; it does not need to be caused by a cause that made it dependent; rather, poverty is necessary to its essence. For everything other than Allah is permanently dependent on Him, not independent of Him for the blink of an eye. This is among the meanings of His name 'al-Samad'. For al-Samad is the One whom everything needs and Who is independent of everything. Just as the richness of the Lord is established for His own sake, not due to a cause, so the poverty of creatures and their need for Him is established for their own essences, not due to a cause that made them dependent on Him. So whoever says that the cause of dependence on the agent is temporal origination, or possibility, or both, if he means that these meanings made the essence poor, then none of that is correct. And if he means that these meanings give knowledge of the poverty of the essence, then it is correct. For each of them implies the poverty of the essence, and it is dependent on Him at the time of its origination and at the time of its persistence; it cannot be independent of Him in either state.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of three views regarding the 'cause of dependence' (illa al-iftiqar) of creatures on the Creator. Ibn Taymiyya first sets up the debate among later theorists: whether the cause is mere possibility (imkan), temporal origination (huduth), or both. He rejects the first view (e.g., Ibn Sina, al-Razi) that mere possibility necessitates dependence, even for an eternal possible. He notes that even Aristotle and earlier philosophers held that anything possible is originated. The second view (e.g., Mu'tazila) that only huduth causes dependence is also rejected. The third view (both) he finds partially acceptable but ultimately insufficient. Ibn Taymiyya's own position is that poverty and need are inherent in the very essence of creatures; they are not caused by imkan or huduth. These are merely indicators (adilla) of that poverty, not its cause. He supports this with the divine name al-Samad, meaning the Independent Who is needed by all. Thus, dependence is a necessary attribute of creatures, not something caused by a secondary attribute. The lesson concludes that imkan and huduth are signs of dependence, not causes.

Key Terms
علة الافتقار
الإمكان
الحدوث
الصمد

According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the correct relationship between imkan (possibility) and the creature's dependence on the Creator?
A) Imkan is the cause that makes the creature dependent.
B) Imkan is a sign that reveals the creature's inherent poverty.
C) Imkan has no relation to dependence.
D) Imkan only applies to originated things.

Key Takeaway
Dependence on Allah is an inseparable part of a creature's essence, not a product of its possibility or origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine a contemporary theologian argues that creatures depend on Allah only because they are created in time. Using Ibn Taymiyya's criteria, explain why this view is invalid and what the correct view is.

Refutation of an Eternal Possible Being
Ibn Taymiyya argues that a possible being cannot exist eternally as a necessary being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain Ibn Taymiyya's three arguments against the coherence of an eternal possible being.

Core Idea
A possible being cannot be eternal because possibility implies capacity for non-existence, contradicting eternal necessity.

Ibn Taymiyya engages in a critical refinement of the philosophical doctrine that a possible being, such as the world, could exist eternally as necessary by another. He argues that this combines contradictory properties: possibility entails the capacity for non-existence, while eternal necessity precludes it. In this lesson, we will unpack his three specific arguments, which target the metaphysical foundations of quiddity, essence, and causality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Locate the claim Ibn Taymiyya is refuting: that a 'possible' (mumkin) can be 'necessary of existence by another' (wajib al-wujud bi-ghayrihi) eternally and perpetually. Note why he calls this a combination of two contradictory (munaqidayn).
The contradiction is between possibility (accepting non-existence) and necessity (not accepting non-existence).
2
Analyse the first argument: rejection of external quiddity
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the idea that a possible being has an essence (mahiyya) in the external world apart from its concrete existence. He argues that in external reality, there is no quiddity that remains when the thing is non-existent; only mental concepts exist. Understand how this undermines the possibility of an eternal possible.
Compare the Mu'tazilite view (al-ma'dum shay') and the philosopher's view of real quiddities, both rejected.
3
Analyse the second argument: the impossibility of non-existence despite distinct essence
Even if one grants a distinct essence for the possible, if existence is eternally necessary for it by another, then non-existence is impossible. The claim that 'in itself it has no existence' does not make it truly possible when necessity is eternal.
This argument shows that eternal necessity overrides any intrinsic possibility.
4
Analyse the third argument: agent must precede effect
Ibn Taymiyya invokes a causal principle: the agent (fa'il) must be temporally prior to its specific effect. This refutes the idea of an eternal effect coexisting with an eternal cause, as the philosophers claim.
This argument is based on a temporal notion of creation, which is a key Ash'ari stance.
5
Assess the overall critique
Consider how all three arguments converge to show that the concept of an eternal possible being is self-contradictory. Does Ibn Taymiyya successfully dismantle the philosophical position?
Note that his critique is internal: he uses the philosophers' own definitions to show inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تقديرُ «ممكن يقبل أن يكون موجوداً، ويقبل أن يكون معدوماً، مع واجب الوجود بغيره أزلاً وأبداً ، فهذا جمع بين المتناقضين. فإن ما يجب وجوده أزلاً وأبداً لا يقبل العدم أصلاً وقول القائل : إنه باعتبار ذاته - مع قطع النظر عن موجبه - يقبل الوجود والعدم باطل لوجوه : الوجه الأول منها : أن هذا مبني على أن له ذاتاً محققة في الخارج غير الموجود المعيَّن، وأن تلك الذات تكون ثابتة مع عدمه وهذا باطل بل ليس له حقيقة في الخارج إلا الموجود الثابت في الخارج، وما يكون حقيقة الوجود لا يقبل العدم أصلاً. فليس في الخارج ماهية ثابتة تكون ثابتة في الخارج في حال العدم حتى يقال : «إن الوجود يعرض لها. ولكن الذات المعلومة المتصوّرة في الذهن تكون تارة موجودة في الخارج وتارةً معدومة . وقد بسط هذا في غير الموضع . فإن هذا يتعلق بقول من يقول: «المعدوم شيء» يعلم في الأذهان من المعتزلة وغيرهم. ويقول من يقول : الماهيات ثابتة في الخارج، وهي الموجودات وما يوجد في المعينة كقول من يقول ذلك من المتفلسفة ومن وافقهم وكلاهما باطل. بل الفرق المعقول هو الفرق بين ما يُعلم في الأذهان وبين ما يوجد في الأعيان، فإذا قيل لما يُعلم في الذهن : «إنه شيء في الذهن أو العلم ، أو ثابت في العلم أو الذهن»، أو سُمي ذلك «ماهية» وقيل إن المثلث تثبت ماهيته في الذهن مع الشك في وجوده فهذا صحيح. وأما إذا قيل: «في الخارج ذات ثابتة لا موجودة» ، أو في الخارج ماهية المثلث أو غيره ثابتة مع أنه ليس موجوداً فهذا باطل يعلم بطلانه بالتصور الجيد السليم والدلائل الكثيرة، كما قد بسط في موضعه . الوجه الثاني: أنه لو فرض أن لـ «الممكن ذاتاً غير وجوده فإذا كان الموجود لازماً لها أبداً وأزلاً واجباً بغيرها لم تقبل هذه الذاتُ أن تكون معدومة قط. وقول القائل : هي في نفسها ليس لها وجود إذا قدر أن هناك ذاتاً غير الوجود لا يقتضي أنه يمكن عدمها ويمكن وجودها مع القول بوجوب وجودها أزلاً وأبداً. الوجه الثالث: أن الفاعل لا بد أن يتقدم مفعوله المعيَّن لا يجوز مقارنته له في الزمان
As for assuming a 'possible that can be existent and can be non-existent, while being necessary of existence by another eternally and perpetually', this is combining two contradictories. For what is necessary of existence eternally and perpetually does not accept non-existence at all. And the statement of the one who says: 'In terms of its essence - disregarding its necessitator - it accepts existence and non-existence' is false for several reasons. The first reason: this is based on the idea that it has a realized essence in the external world other than the specified existent, and that that essence is established with its non-existence, and this is false. Rather, it has no reality in the external except the existent that is established in the external, and whatever is a reality of existence does not accept non-existence at all. So there is no quiddity in the external established in the external when non-existent such that it is said 'existence accedes to it'. Rather, the known essence conceived in the mind is sometimes existent in the external and sometimes non-existent. And this has been elaborated elsewhere. For this relates to the statement of those who say 'the non-existent is a thing' known in minds, among the Mu'tazila and others; and the statement of those who say quiddities are established in the external—they are the existents and what is existent in the particular, like the statement of those who say that among the philosophers and those who agree with them—both are false. Rather, the rational distinction is the distinction between what is known in minds and what is found in external entities. So if it is said of what is known in the mind 'it is a thing in the mind or knowledge, or established in knowledge or the mind', or that is called 'quiddity', and it is said that the quiddity of the triangle is established in the mind while doubting its existence, then this is correct. But if it is said 'in the external there is an established essence that is not existent', or 'in the external the quiddity of the triangle or other is established while it is not existent', this is false, whose falsehood is known by sound, correct conception and many proofs, as has been elaborated in its place. The second reason: even if it is assumed that the possible has an essence distinct from its existence, then if existence is necessary for it always and eternally as necessary by another, this essence could never be non-existent. And the statement of the one who says 'in itself it has no existence' when it is assumed that there is an essence other than existence does not entail that it can be non-existent and can be existent while declaring its existence necessary eternally and perpetually. The third reason: the agent must precede its specific effect; it is not permissible for it to be simultaneous with it in time.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophical claim that a possible being (such as the world in some philosophical systems) could exist eternally as a necessary being by virtue of another. Ibn Taymiyya argues that this is a contradiction: a possible being by its own essence cannot be necessary even by another in an eternal sense, because necessity of existence implies impossibility of non-existence, whereas possibility implies ability to be non-existent. He then presents three explicit arguments. The first argument attacks the notion of an external essence distinct from existence, a view held by some Mu'tazila (who say the non-existent is a thing) and philosophers (who claim quiddities are real in the external). Ibn Taymiyya insists that nothing exists in the external except the concrete existent; what is known in the mind is a mental concept. Therefore, there is no substratum for possibility that remains when the thing is non-existent. The second argument: even if one grants a distinct essence, if existence is eternally necessary for it, then non-existence is impossible; thus the notion of 'possible in itself' becomes meaningless. The third argument is temporal: an agent must precede its effect, so an eternal effect coexisting with its cause is impossible. This is a direct critique of the philosophers' claim of an eternal world necessarily caused by God.

Key Terms
ممكن
واجب الوجود بغيره
ماهية
الذات المعلومة المتصورة في الذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The agent must precede the effect, so an eternal effect is impossible.
B) The assumption of an external essence distinct from existence is false; only mental concepts exist when the thing is absent.
C) Necessity of existence allows for non-existence if the cause changes.
D) The possible being's essence is eternal and does not require a cause.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the eternal possible being by exposing the contradiction between eternal necessity and genuine possibility, using arguments against external quiddities, distinct essences, and simultaneity of cause and effect.

Critique of Temporal Precedence of Agent Over Effect
Refutes temporal precedence of agent over effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ambiguity in the term 'illa (cause) and distinguish between efficient cause (fa'il) and condition (shart) in arguments about temporal precedence.

Core Idea
The agent does not always temporally precede its effect; conditions can be simultaneous.

Logicians often claim that the agent must precede its effect in time, citing examples like the hand moving a ring. Ibn Taymiyya dissects this claim, showing that what they call a 'cause' is often merely a condition. Understanding this distinction is key to avoiding confusion in causal arguments, especially in theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Read the passage and find the claim that the agent must precede its effect. They give the example of hand movement preceding ring movement, calling it causal precedence.
This claim is from the logicians, not Ibn Taymiyya's own view.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He denies that this example involves an efficient cause at all. Instead, he calls it a condition (shart) for the effect. Conditions can be simultaneous with the conditioned.
The hand movement is not the efficient cause of the ring's movement; both are effects of a single agent (the person).
3
Distinguish efficient cause from condition
An efficient cause (fa'il) actively produces its effect and temporally precedes it. A condition (shart) is something without which the effect cannot exist, but it does not produce it; both can coexist in time.
Example: the existence of the father is a condition for the son's existence, but the father does not temporally precede the son in the sense of efficient causation.
4
Recognize the ambiguity of 'illa
Ibn Taymiyya points out that the term 'illa (cause) is used ambiguously to mean 'agent', 'receptacle', or 'condition'. The logicians conflate these meanings, leading to false conclusions.
This ambiguity is the root of the confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه الثالث : إن الفاعل لا بد أن يتقدم مفعوله المعين وما يذكرونه من تقدم حركة اليد على حركة الخاتم ونحو ذلك، ويجعلونه تقدماً بالعلة ليس في شيء من ذلك علة فاعلة أصلاً، وإنما ذلك شرط في هذا. ولا يمكن أحدا أن يبين في الوجود علة فاعلة لمعلول مفعول مع مقارنتها له في الزمان أصلاً، وإنما يمكن المقارنة بين الشرط والمشروط . ولكن لفظ «العلة» فيه إجمال - يراد به «الفاعل»، المفعول لفاعله في الزمان ویراد به «القابل» و «الشروط». وهذا أيضاً مما حَصَل فيه تلبيس في صفات واجب الوجود» لَمَّا قالوا : «لو كانت له صفات لكانت معلولة للذات والواجب لا يكون معلولاً». فيقال لهم: «واجب الوجود قد يُعنى به ما لا يحتاج إلى فاعل، فالصفات واجبة بهذا الاعتبار . وقد يعنى به ما لا يفتقر إلى محل، وعلى هذا فالذات واجبة وأما الصفات فليست واجبة بهذا التفسير. والبرهان قام على أن الممكنات لا بد لها من فاعل لا يفتقر إلى ما سواه، لم يقم على أن صفاته كذاته لا تفتقر إلى محل . وهذه الأمور مبسوطة في موضعها .
The Fourth Station - The Third Aspect: That the agent must precede its specific effect. And what they mention of the precedence of the hand's movement over the ring's movement and the like, making it a precedence by cause, none of that is an efficient cause at all; rather that is a condition for this. And no one can ever demonstrate in existence an efficient cause for an effect while it is simultaneous with it in time at all; rather simultaneity is possible only between the condition and the conditioned. But the term 'cause' is ambiguous: it is used to mean 'the agent', the effect is temporally after its agent, and it is used to mean 'the receptacle' and 'the conditions'. This too is a point where confusion has occurred regarding the attributes of the Necessary Existent, when they said: 'If He had attributes, they would be effects of the essence, and the Necessary Existent cannot be an effect.' So it is said to them: 'Necessary Existent' may mean that which does not need a maker; the attributes are necessary in this sense. And it may mean that which does not depend on a substrate; on this interpretation, the essence is necessary, but the attributes are not necessary. And the proof has been established that contingent things must have a maker that does not depend on anything else; it has not been established that His attributes, like His essence, do not depend on a substrate. These matters are elaborated in their proper place.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that the agent (fa'il) must precede its effect in time. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating the objection: they argue that the movement of the hand precedes the movement of the ring, and they consider this a causal precedence (taqaddum bil-'illa). He denies that this is an efficient cause; rather it is a condition (shart) for the effect. He then argues that no one can show an efficient cause that is simultaneous with its effect in time; simultaneity only applies to condition and conditioned. The core of his critique is the ambiguity in the word 'illa (cause): it can mean agent, receptacle (qabil), or condition. He then applies this to the theological issue of God's attributes (sifat). The logicians say: if God had attributes, they would be caused by the essence, but the Necessary Existent cannot be caused. Ibn Taymiyya replies by clarifying that 'necessary existent' (wajib al-wujud) can have two meanings: (1) that which does not need a creator, in which case the attributes are necessary; (2) that which does not depend on a substrate (mahall), in which case only the essence is necessary, not the attributes. He insists that the proof for the necessity of a maker for contingent things does not establish that God's attributes are independent of a substrate. This passage shows Ibn Taymiyya's method of distinguishing term meanings to dissolve apparent contradictions.

Key Terms
علة
فاعل
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the error in the logicians' example of hand and ring movement?
A) They confuse efficient cause with condition.
B) They think the ring moves by itself.
C) They deny that the hand moves first.
D) They use a physical example in a theological argument.

Key Takeaway
The term 'cause' ('illa) is ambiguous; not everything that precedes an effect is an efficient cause—it may be a condition, which can be simultaneous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the example of a teacher writing on a board: the teacher's hand movement causes the writing. Analyze whether this is an efficient cause or a condition, and whether the hand movement temporally precedes the writing. Identify the ambiguity in the term 'cause' here.
?
In Kalam, some argue that God's attribute of speech (kalam) must be eternal and not created. Using Ibn Taymiyya's distinction, explain whether God's speech is an effect of His essence or a condition for creation.

Two Meanings of Wajib al-Wujud and the Attributes of God
Resolves objection about God's attributes using two meanings of wajib al-wujud

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Ibn Taymiyya's dual interpretation of 'wajib al-wujud' to resolve the objection that God's attributes would be caused and thus impossible.

Core Idea
'Wajib al-wujud' can mean 'needs no creator' or 'needs no substrate'; attributes are necessary in the first sense but not the second.

The logicians argue that if God had attributes, they would be effects of the essence, but the Necessary Existent cannot be an effect. Ibn Taymiyya counters by clarifying that 'necessary existent' can have two meanings. This lesson shows how his semantic distinction dissolves the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' objection
The logicians say: if God has attributes (like knowledge, power), these attributes would be caused by the essence, making the essence a cause and the attributes effects. But the Necessary Existent cannot be an effect, so God cannot have attributes.
This objection is based on the assumption that attributes are distinct entities from the essence.
2
Find Ibn Taymiyya's response
He responds by distinguishing two meanings of 'wajib al-wujud'. First meaning: that which does not need a maker (fa'il). Second meaning: that which does not depend on a substrate (mahall).
This is not a mere terminological exercise; it shows the objection equivocates on 'wajib al-wujud'.
3
Apply the first meaning to attributes
Under the first meaning, the attributes are necessary because they do not need a creator separate from God. They are necessary in the sense that they are not contingent upon an external agent.
Thus, they are not 'effects' in the sense of being created by an external cause.
4
Apply the second meaning to essence vs attributes
Under the second meaning, only the essence is necessary (does not depend on a substrate), while the attributes inhere in the essence and thus depend on the essence as a substrate. So they are not necessary in this sense.
This preserves the distinction between essence and attributes without making them contingent in the sense of needing a maker.
5
See how the objection dissolves
The logicians' argument conflates the two meanings: they assume that if attributes need a substrate, they must also need a maker. Ibn Taymiyya shows that needing a substrate does not imply needing a maker; the proof for contingent things requiring a maker does not extend to attributes.
This is a classic example of resolving a theological problem by semantic analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه الثالث : إن الفاعل لا بد أن يتقدم مفعوله المعين وما يذكرونه من تقدم حركة اليد على حركة الخاتم ونحو ذلك، ويجعلونه تقدماً بالعلة ليس في شيء من ذلك علة فاعلة أصلاً، وإنما ذلك شرط في هذا. ولا يمكن أحدا أن يبين في الوجود علة فاعلة لمعلول مفعول مع مقارنتها له في الزمان أصلاً، وإنما يمكن المقارنة بين الشرط والمشروط . ولكن لفظ «العلة» فيه إجمال - يراد به «الفاعل»، المفعول لفاعله في الزمان ویراد به «القابل» و «الشروط». وهذا أيضاً مما حَصَل فيه تلبيس في صفات واجب الوجود» لَمَّا قالوا : «لو كانت له صفات لكانت معلولة للذات والواجب لا يكون معلولاً». فيقال لهم: «واجب الوجود قد يُعنى به ما لا يحتاج إلى فاعل، فالصفات واجبة بهذا الاعتبار . وقد يعنى به ما لا يفتقر إلى محل، وعلى هذا فالذات واجبة وأما الصفات فليست واجبة بهذا التفسير. والبرهان قام على أن الممكنات لا بد لها من فاعل لا يفتقر إلى ما سواه، لم يقم على أن صفاته كذاته لا تفتقر إلى محل . وهذه الأمور مبسوطة في موضعها .
The Fourth Station - The Third Aspect: That the agent must precede its specific effect. And what they mention of the precedence of the hand's movement over the ring's movement and the like, making it a precedence by cause, none of that is an efficient cause at all; rather that is a condition for this. And no one can ever demonstrate in existence an efficient cause for an effect while it is simultaneous with it in time at all; rather simultaneity is possible only between the condition and the conditioned. But the term 'cause' is ambiguous: it is used to mean 'the agent', the effect is temporally after its agent, and it is used to mean 'the receptacle' and 'the conditions'. This too is a point where confusion has occurred regarding the attributes of the Necessary Existent, when they said: 'If He had attributes, they would be effects of the essence, and the Necessary Existent cannot be an effect.' So it is said to them: 'Necessary Existent' may mean that which does not need a maker; the attributes are necessary in this sense. And it may mean that which does not depend on a substrate; on this interpretation, the essence is necessary, but the attributes are not necessary. And the proof has been established that contingent things must have a maker that does not depend on anything else; it has not been established that His attributes, like His essence, do not depend on a substrate. These matters are elaborated in their proper place.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the logicians' claim that the agent (fa'il) must precede its effect in time. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating the objection: they argue that the movement of the hand precedes the movement of the ring, and they consider this a causal precedence (taqaddum bil-'illa). He denies that this is an efficient cause; rather it is a condition (shart) for the effect. He then argues that no one can show an efficient cause that is simultaneous with its effect in time; simultaneity only applies to condition and conditioned. The core of his critique is the ambiguity in the word 'illa (cause): it can mean agent, receptacle (qabil), or condition. He then applies this to the theological issue of God's attributes (sifat). The logicians say: if God had attributes, they would be caused by the essence, but the Necessary Existent cannot be caused. Ibn Taymiyya replies by clarifying that 'necessary existent' (wajib al-wujud) can have two meanings: (1) that which does not need a creator, in which case the attributes are necessary; (2) that which does not depend on a substrate (mahall), in which case only the essence is necessary, not the attributes. He insists that the proof for the necessity of a maker for contingent things does not establish that God's attributes are independent of a substrate. This passage shows Ibn Taymiyya's method of distinguishing term meanings to dissolve apparent contradictions.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
صفات
فاعل
محل

B) They conflate two meanings of 'wajib al-wujud'.
C) They think attributes are temporally after essence.
D) They ignore the proof for contingent things.

Key Takeaway
The objection that God cannot have attributes because they would be effects is dissolved by clarifying that 'necessary existent' can mean independence from a creator, not necessarily from a substrate.

Why Logical Syllogism Fails for Allah: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique
Author argues that logical syllogism cannot apply to Allah because He has no equal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to articulate Ibn Taymiyya's argument that logical syllogism cannot give specific knowledge about Allah due to His absolute uniqueness.

Core Idea
Logical syllogism assumes resemblance; Allah has no likeness, so it fails.

Ibn Taymiyya targets the claim that Aristotelian syllogism (qiyas) can be applied universally, even to Allah. He argues that because the syllogism relies on similarity between terms, it cannot capture what is unique about the Creator. Let's examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' assumption
What do logicians claim about qiyas? They believe it is a universal tool valid for all subjects, including theological ones. Note that they assume equality among individuals (tasaawi al-afraad) and resemblance of branch to root (tamaathul).
2
Pinpoint Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He states that Allah has no likeness (la mithla lahu). Therefore the assumption of similarity fails. This is the foundational objection: the syllogism's mechanism depends on comparison, but with Allah there is no comparable entity.
3
Trace the consequence: only universal knowledge
If qiyas is used for Allah, it can only produce a universal concept shared between Allah and others (e.g., 'creator' in general). It does not indicate what is specific to Allah (e.g., His essence or unique attributes).
Ibn Taymiyya says the qiyas yields 'amran kulliyyan mushtatakan' – a universal common matter.
4
Explain the need for additional knowledge
Even if that universal is instantiated only by Allah (i.e., the type is confined to one individual), the syllogism alone does not specify that individual. To know that Allah is the referent, one needs external knowledge ('ilm akhar).
This highlights an epistemological limit: qiyas cannot produce determinate knowledge of unique beings.
5
Evaluate the critique's force
Is Ibn Taymiyya's objection valid? Consider whether any syllogism about Allah can be framed without assuming similarity. For example, 'Allah is eternal; everything eternal is uncreated' – does 'eternal' apply univocally? His critique challenges the univocity of terms used for Allah.
This step encourages reflection on the broader implications for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكَذَلِكَ فِي إِثْبَاتِ صِفَاتِهِ، وَإِثْبَاتِ النُّبُوَّةِ وَالْمَعَادِ؛ كَمَا قَدْ بُسِطَ فِي مَوْضِعِهِ. وَأَمَّا «الْقِيَاسُ» الَّذِي تَسْتَوِي أَفْرَادُهُ وَيُمَاثِلُ الْفَرْعُ فِيهِ أَصْلَهُ، فَهَذَا يَمْتَنِعُ اسْتِعْمَالُ الْقِيَاسِ الْمَنْطِقِيِّ فِي حَقِّ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا مِثْلَ لَهُ سُبْحَانَهُ وَتَعَالَى. وَإِذَا اسْتُعْمِلَ فِيهِ مِثْلُ هَذَا الْقِيَاسِ فِي حَقِّهِ لَمْ يُفِدْ إِلَّا أَمْرًا كُلِّيًا مُشْتَرَكًا بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ غَيْرِهِ، لَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى مَا يَخْتَصُّ بِهِ الرَّبُّ سُبْحَانَهُ، إِلَّا أَنْ يُضَمَّ إِلَيْهِ عِلْمٌ آخَرُ. فَإِنَّ هَذَا الْكُلِّيَّ الَّذِي هُوَ مَدْلُولُ الْقِيَاسِ قَدِ انْحَصَرَ نَوْعُهُ فِي شَخْصِهِ، وَهَذَا أَيْضًا لَا يُفِيدُ التَّعْيِينَ، بَلْ لَا بُدَّ فِي التَّعْيِينِ مِنْ عِلْمٍ آخَرَ.
And similarly in establishing His attributes, and establishing prophethood and the afterlife; as has been elaborated in its place. As for the 'qiyas' (syllogism) whose individuals are equal and in which the branch resembles its root, then it is impossible to use logical syllogism with respect to Allah the Exalted, for Allah has no likeness, glorified and exalted be He. And if such qiyas is used with respect to Him, it yields only a universal matter common between Him and other than Him, not indicating what is specific to the Lord, unless another knowledge is joined to it. For this universal, which is the meaning of the qiyas, has its type confined to its individual, and this also does not give specification; rather specification requires another knowledge.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a critique by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' claim that logical syllogism (qiyas) can be applied universally, including to Allah. He argues that the qiyas they use is based on equality of individuals and resemblance of branch to root (tamathul), implying a similarity between the subject and predicate terms. However, Allah is unique and has no likeness (laysa ka-mithlihi shay'). Therefore, such qiyas cannot produce knowledge specific to Allah; it only yields a universal concept shared with other beings. To know Allah's specific attributes, one needs additional knowledge outside the syllogism. This is a fundamental epistemological critique: the universal generated by qiyas is insufficient for specifying the unique reality of Allah. Ibn Taymiyya points out that even if the universal's type is confined to a single individual (Allah), the syllogism itself does not indicate that individual specifically; external knowledge is required. This highlights the limits of Aristotelian logic in theology.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): In this passage, the logical syllogism that relies on equality of individuals and resemblance of branch to root. [Note: Ibn Taymiyya critiques this, while logicians view it as valid inference.]
مثل (mithl): Likeness or equal; Allah has no mithl.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that logical syllogism, which depends on similarity, cannot yield specific knowledge of Allah; it only yields universal concepts, requiring additional knowledge for specification.

Knowledge Without Qiyas: Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Logicians' Exclusivity Claim
Ibn Taymiyya argues against the necessity of qiyas for certain knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that the same knowledge can be obtained through different epistemic methods and apply it to critique the claim that qiyas is necessary for certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not tied to a single method; the same truth can be known by sense, report, or reasoning depending on the knower.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks the logicians' assumption that some propositions can only be known through syllogistic demonstration. He argues that knowledge is not a property of the proposition itself but varies according to the knower's circumstances. By giving concrete examples from prophethood, the crescent moon, and everyday knowledge, he shows that multiple paths lead to the same truth. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim being attacked
Find the logicians' claim that Ibn Taymiyya is refuting. Look for the assertion that certain knowledge requires qiyas.
The logicians assert that some propositions can only be known through syllogism.
2
Extract the examples and their methods
List the examples Ibn Taymiyya gives (prophethood, crescent moon) and the different ways each can be known: sense, tawatur, calculation, inference.
Notice that each example has multiple methods, not just qiyas.
3
Understand the principle of epistemic plurality
Recognize the key sentence: 'So a single known thing is known by this person through sense, by this person through report, and by this person through reasoning.' This shows that knowledge depends on the knower, not the proposition.
This principle undermines the idea that qiyas is the only or necessary path.
4
Apply the principle to universal propositions
Ibn Taymiyya extends the argument from particular cases to universal propositions. Some universals are known without qiyas or proof, and this varies by person.
The condition of the knower, not the proposition, determines the method.
5
Evaluate the conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that claiming 'what Zayd knows by qiyas cannot be known otherwise' is a false negation. Test this: does the logicians' claim hold given the examples?
The argument uses counterexamples to refute a universal claim, a valid logical move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه السادس من القضايا ما يمكن العلم به بغير توسط «القياس» بالتواتر؛ ويُعلم ذلك بالاستدلال، فإنه ادعى النبوة وأتى بما يناقض النبوة، فيعلم بالاستدلال. وكذلك الهلال، قد يُعلم طلوعه بالرؤية فتكون القضية حسّية؛ ويعلم ذلك مَنْ لم يره بالأخبار المتواترة فتكون القضية عنده من المتواترات؛ ويعلم ذلك من علم أن تلك الليلة إحدى وثلاثون بالحساب والاستدلال ومثل هذا كثير. فالمعلوم الواحد يعلمه هذا بالحس، وهذا بالخبر، وهذا بالنظر. وهذه طرق العلم لبني آدم. وهكذا القضايا الكلية إذا كان منها ما يُعلم بلا قياس ولا دليل، وليس لذلك حدّ نفس القضايا بل ذلك بحسب أحوال بني آدم لم يمكن أن يقال فيما علمه زيد بالقياس إنه لا يمكن غيره أن يعلمه بلا قياس. بل هذا نفي كاذب.
Fourth Station - The Sixth Aspect: Of the propositions, what can be known without the mediation of qiyas by tawatur; and it is known by inference, for he claimed prophethood and brought what contradicts prophethood, so it is known by inference. Likewise the crescent: its rising may be known by sight, so the proposition is sensory; and the one who did not see it knows it through mutawatir reports, so the proposition is among the mutawatirat for him; and it is known by one who knows that that night is the thirty-first by calculation and inference. And much of this is abundant. So a single known thing is known by this person through sense, by this person through report, and by this person through reasoning. And these are the methods of knowledge for the sons of Adam. And thus the universal propositions: if there are some of them that are known without qiyas and without proof, and there is no limit for that in the propositions themselves but rather it is according to the conditions of the sons of Adam, it cannot be said that what Zayd knew by qiyas cannot be known by another without qiyas. Rather, this is a false negation.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the claim that some propositions can only be known through qiyas (syllogism). Ibn Taymiyya opens the sixth aspect of the fourth station by asserting that knowledge can be attained without qiyas through tawatur (recurrent reports). He gives the example of prophethood: one may know it through inference from miracles, but also through tawatur. Similarly, the crescent moon can be known by sight, by tawatur for those who didn't see it, or by calculation. Thus, a single known thing can be known by different methods depending on the person. He extends this to universal propositions: some are known without qiyas or proof, and this is not a fixed property of the proposition but varies with the knower. Therefore, it is false to claim that what Zayd knows through qiyas cannot be known by another person without qiyas. The argument undermines the logicians' assumption that certain knowledge requires syllogistic demonstration.

القياس
التواتر
الاستدلال

D) By qiyas

Key Takeaway
No single epistemic method owns a proposition; knowledge is relative to the knower's circumstances, making the claim that qiyas is necessary for certain knowledge a false negation.

Examples Strengthen Universal Knowledge: Ibn Taymiyya's Rebuttal
Ibn Taymiyya argues examples strengthen universal knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that examples strengthen universal knowledge and identify his critique of the logicians' contrary claim.

Core Idea
Universal knowledge is built on particulars; examples are its foundation, not its weakness.

Ibn Taymiyya directly challenges the logicians' assertion that mentioning examples weakens a syllogism. He argues that the universal common (المشترك الكلي) is more firmly established in the mind when accompanied by particular instances. This lesson unpacks his key argument and its implications for epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim under attack
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states the logicians' position that mentioning examples weakens the syllogism. He quotes them implicitly.
The claim is that examples are detrimental to logical reasoning.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's counter-claim
Read his opening sentence: the universal common with some of its individuals is more established in the intellect than when mentioned alone.
This directly opposes the logicians' view.
3
Find his supporting examples
Identify the natural phenomena he uses: heat/cold in bodies and steam rising. Note how he says these examples strengthen knowledge of the universal principle.
The examples are not just illustrations but reinforce the universal.
4
Understand the intellectual property involved
Locate the phrase 'خاصة العقل' and interpret it: the intellect knows universals through particulars.
This is the epistemological foundation of his argument.
5
Assess the critique's strength
Evaluate whether Ibn Taymiyya successfully shows that examples are necessary, not detrimental. Consider his use of scriptural evidence at the end.
The argument is coherent and supported by multiple examples.

المقام الرابع - الوجه السابع : ذكر الأصل لتنبيه العقل على المشترك الكلي المشترك مع بعض أفراده أثبتُ في العقل من ذكره مجرداً عن جميع الأفراد باتفاق العقلاء. ولهذا هم يقولون : إن العقل بحسب إحساسه [٣٥٨] بالجزئيات يدرك العقل بينها قدراً مشتركاً كلياً. فالكليات في النفس تقع بعد معرفة الجزئيات المعيَّنة. فمعرفة الكليات الجزئيات المعينة من أعظم الأسباب في معرفة الكليات فكيف يكون ذكرها مضعفاً للقياس، ويكون عدم ذكرها موجباً لقوته؟ وهذه خاصة العقل، فإن خاصة العقل معرفته الكليات بتوسط معرفته بالجزئيات فمن أنكرها أنكر خاصة عقل الإنسان، ومن جعل ذكرها بدون شيء من محالها المعينة أقوى من ذكرها مع التمثيل لمواضعها المعينة كان مكابراً. والعقلاء باتفاقهم يعلمون أن ضرب المثل الكلي مما يعين على معرفته، وأنه ليس الحال إذا ذكر مع المثال كالحال إذا ذكر مجرداً عن المثال . ومن تدبر جميع ما يتكلم فيه الناس من الكليات المعلومة بالعقل في الطب، أمثلة لكلية انجذاب النظير والحساب والطبيعيات والصناعات والتجارات وغير ذلك، وجد الأمر كذلك. فإذا قيل : يَسْخُن جوف الإنسان في الشتاء ويبرد في الصيف، لأنه في الشتاء يكون الهواء بارداً فيبرد ظاهرُ البدن فتهرب الحرارة إلى باطن البدن، لأن الضد يهرب من الضد، والنظير ينجذب إلى شبيهه، فتظهر البرودة إلى الظاهر، لأن شبيه الشيء منجذب إليه - كان هذا أمراً كلياً مطلقاً فإذا قيل : ولهذا يَسْخُن جوفُ الأرض في الشتاء، [٣٥٩] وجوف الحيوان كله، ولهذا تبرد الأجواف في الصيف لسخونة الظواهر فتهرب البرودة إلى الأجواف - كان كلما تصور الإنسان النظائر قويت معرفته بتلك الكلية، وهو أن الضد يهرب من ضده والنظير ينجذب إلى نظيره. وهذا معلوم في الطبيعيات والنفسانيات وغيرهما. لكن إذا ذكرت النظائر قوي العلم بذلك. وقد يعبر عن ذلك بأن «الجنسية علة القم. [احتمال: علة الحب]». وكذلك إذا قيل : الشمس إذا وقعت على البحر أو غيره سَخَّنته فتصاعد منه بخار، مثال تصاعد البخار لأن الحرارة تحلل الرطوبة. ثم قيل : كما يتصاعد البخار من القدر التي فيها الماء الحار - / كان هذا المثال مما يؤكد معرفة الأول. والأقيسة التي يستعملها الفلاسفة في علومهم ويجعلونها كلية كلُّها يعتضدون فيها على الشاهد بالأمثلة. وليس مع القوم إلا ما علموه من صفات الأمور المشاهدة، ثم قاسوا الغائب على المشاهدة به بالجامع المشترك الذي يجعلونه كلياً. فإن لم يكن هذا صحيحاً لم يكن مع أحد من أهل الأرض علم كلي يشترك فيه ما شهده وما غاب عنه، حتى قوله : «الخبز يشبع و الماء يروي ونحو ذلك . فإنه لم يعلم بحسه إلا أموراً معينة، فمن أين له أن الغائب بمنزلة الشاهد إلا بهذه الطريق؟ والإنسان قد ينكر أمراً حتى يرى واحداً جنسه ، فيقر بالنوع ويستفيد بذلك حكماً كلياً. ولهذا يقول سبحانه: ﴿ كَذَّبَتْ قَوْمُ [٣٦٠] نُوحٍ الْمُرْسَلِينَ، كَذَبَتْ عَادُ الْمُرْسَلِينَ، كَذَّبَتْ ثَمُودُ الْمُرْسَلِينَ ﴾ ، ونحو ذلك . وكلّ من هؤلاء إنما جاءه رسول واحد، لكن كانوا مكذبين بجنس الرسل بجنس الرُّسل لم يكن تكذيبهم بالواحد لخصوصه. وهذا بخلاف تكذيب اليهود والنصارى لمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فإنهم لم يكذبوا جنس الرسل، إنما كَذَّبوا واحداً بعينه. بخلاف مشركي العرب الذين لم يعرفوا الرسل، فإن الله يحتج عليهم في القرآن بإثبات جنس الرسالة . ولهذا يجيب سبحانه عن شُبَهِ منكري جنس الرسالة كقولهم : لو أَبَعَثَ الله بشرا رسولا [سورة الإسراء : ٩٤]، فيقول : ﴿وَمَا أَرْسَلْنَا مِن قَبْلِكَ إِلَّا رِجَالًا نُوحِيَ إِلَيْهِمْ فَسْتَلُوا أَهْلَ الذِكرِ إِن كُنتُمْ لَا تَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ [سورة النحل : ٤٣] . أي هذا متواتر عند أهل الكتاب، فاسئلوهم عن الرسل الذين جاءتهم أكانوا بشراً أم لا؟». وكذلك قوله : ﴿ وَقَالُوا لَوْلَا أُنزِلَ عَلَيْهِ مَلَكُ وَلَوْ أَنزَلْنَا مَلَكًا لَّقُضِيَ الْأَمْرُ ثُمَّ لَا يُنظَرُونَ ) وَلَوْ جَعَلْنَهُ مَلَكًا لَّجَعَلْتَهُ رَجُلًا وَلَلَبَسْنَا عَلَيْهِم مَّا يَلْبِسُونَ ﴾ [سورة الأنعام: ۸ - ۹] . فإنهم لا يُطيقون الأخذ عن الملك في صورته ، فلو أرسلنا إليهم مَلَكاً لجعلناه رجلاً في صورة الإنسان، وحينئذ كان يلتبس عليهم الأمرُ ويقولون هو رجل ، والرجل لا يكون رسولاً». وكذلك الرسل قبله. قال تعالى : ( أَوَعَبْتُمْ أَن جَاءَكُمْ ذِكْرٌ مِّن رَّبِّكُمْ عَلَى رَجُلٍ مِّنكُمْ لِيُنذِرَكُمْ) [سورة الأعراف : ٦٣] ، كما قال [٣٦١] تعالى: ﴿ أَكَانَ لِلنَّاسِ عَجَبًا أَنْ أَوْحَيْنَا إِلَى رَجُلٍ مِّنْهُمْ أَنْ أَنذِرِ النَّاسَ ﴾ [سورة يونس : ٢] وكما قال تعالى: ﴿قُلْ مَا كُنتُ بِدْعَا مِنَ الرُّسُلِ ﴾ [سورة الأحقاف: ٩]، ونحو ذلك.
The fourth station - the seventh aspect: Mentioning the principle to alert the intellect to the universal common. The universal common with some of its individuals is more established in the intellect than mentioning it devoid of all individuals, by agreement of the rational people. And therefore they say: The intellect, by virtue of its sensation of particulars, perceives among them a common universal quantity. So universals occur in the soul after knowledge of specific particulars. Knowledge of universals through specific particulars is among the greatest causes for knowledge of universals; so how can mentioning them weaken the syllogism, and not mentioning them be a cause for its strength? And this is a property of the intellect, for the property of the intellect is its knowledge of universals through its knowledge of particulars. So whoever denies this denies the property of the human intellect, and whoever makes mentioning them without any of their specific instances stronger than mentioning them with exemplification of their specific loci is being obstinate. The rational people unanimously know that striking a universal example assists in its knowledge, and that the case when it is mentioned with an example is not like the case when it is mentioned without an example. And whoever ponders all that people speak of regarding universals known by the intellect in medicine, examples for the universal of attraction of like and calculation and natural sciences and crafts and trades and other than that, finds the matter so. So if it is said: The interior of man becomes hot in winter and cold in summer, because in winter the air is cold so the exterior of the body cools and the heat flees to the interior of the body, for the opposite flees from the opposite, and the like is attracted to its similar, so coldness appears to the exterior because the similar of a thing is attracted to it - this would be an absolute universal matter. Then if it is said: Therefore the interior of the earth becomes hot in winter, and the interior of all animals, and therefore the interiors become cold in summer due to the heat of the exteriors so the coldness flees to the interiors - then whenever a person imagines the similars, his knowledge of that universal strengthens, i.e., that the opposite flees from its opposite and the like is attracted to its like. And this is known in natural and psychological matters and others. But when the similars are mentioned, knowledge of that is strengthened. And this is sometimes expressed by 'Homogeneity is the cause of [love?]'. Likewise, if it is said: The sun when it falls on the sea or other than it heats it so steam rises from it, an example of the rising of steam because heat dissolves moisture. Then it is said: Just as steam rises from the pot containing hot water - then this example strengthens knowledge of the first. And the syllogisms that the philosophers use in their sciences and make them universal - all of them they seek support in them by sensory evidence with examples. And the people have nothing except what they know of the attributes of observed matters, then they analogize the absent to the observed by the common factor that they make universal. For if this were not correct, there would be with no one on earth a universal knowledge that encompasses what he observed and what is absent from him, even his statement: 'Bread satiates and water quenches' and so on. For he only knows by his sensation specific matters; so from where does he have that the absent is like the observed except by this method? And a person may deny a matter until he sees one of its kind, then he acknowledges the type and thereby benefits a universal judgment. And therefore Allah Almighty says: 'The people of Noah denied the messengers, 'Ad denied the messengers, Thamud denied the messengers' and so on. And each of these was only sent one messenger, but they were deniers of the genus of messengers; their denial was not of the one for his specificity. This is unlike the denial of the Jews and Christians of Muhammad صلى الله عليه وسلم, for they did not deny the genus of messengers, they only denied one specific one. Unlike the polytheist Arabs who did not know messengers, for Allah argues against them in the Qur'an by establishing the genus of messengerhood. And therefore He answers the doubts of the deniers of the genus of messengerhood, like their statement: 'If Allah had sent a human as a messenger' [Surah al-Isra 94], He says 'And We sent not before you except men whom We inspired - so ask the people of remembrance if you do not know' [Surah al-Nahl 43]. That is, this is mutawatir among the People of the Book, so ask them about the messengers who came to them, were they human or not? And likewise His statement: 'And they said: Why was there not sent down to him an angel? And if We had sent down an angel, the matter would have been decided and then they would not be reprieved. And if We had made him an angel, We would have made him a man and We would have confused for them what they confuse' [Surah al-An'am 8-9]. For they cannot receive from an angel in its form; if We had sent them an angel, We would have made him a man in the form of a human, and then the matter would be confused for them and they would say he is a man, and a man cannot be a messenger. And likewise the messengers before him. He said: 'Is it a wonder for people that We inspired a man from among them to warn the people?' [Surah Yunus 2]. And as He said: 'Say: I am not an innovation among the messengers' [Surah al-Ahqaf 9], and so on.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against the logicians' claim that mentioning examples weakens the syllogism. Ibn Taymiyya opens by stating that a universal common (المشترك الكلي) when accompanied by some of its individuals is more firmly established in the intellect than when mentioned in isolation. He then explains that the intellect grasps universals by first sensing particulars; thus, universals follow knowledge of specific individuals. Therefore, mentioning particulars cannot weaken the syllogism; rather, it strengthens it. He calls those who claim the opposite as obstinate (مكابر). He provides examples from natural phenomena: the movement of heat and cold in bodies, and the rise of steam from heated water. He also cites the use of examples in various fields like medicine, calculation, and crafts. Next, he argues that the philosophers' syllogisms rely on observed examples; all universal knowledge depends on extrapolating from the observed to the absent. Without this method, even basic knowledge like 'bread satiates' would be impossible. Finally, he uses a scriptural example: the denial of a single messenger by past nations constitutes denial of the genus of messengers (جنس الرسل), showing that the universal genus is known through the particular instance. This reinforces his point that universal knowledge is achieved through particulars and examples.

Key Terms
المشترك الكلي
التمثيل
القياس
خاصة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, why does mentioning examples strengthen universal knowledge?
A) Because examples add sensory evidence that the intellect uses to grasp the universal.
B) Because examples confuse the intellect by mixing universal and particular.
C) Because the logicians said so.
D) Because examples are more important than universals.

Key Takeaway
Examples are not a distraction; they are the very means by which universal knowledge is confirmed and strengthened in the intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Take a universal statement in fiqh, e.g., 'Every harmful act is prohibited.' Use Ibn Taymiyya's method to strengthen it by mentioning specific examples of harmful acts from the Qur'an or Sunnah. Write two sentences showing how the examples reinforce the universal principle.
?
Task 2: Identify a claim made by a contemporary speaker that a general Islamic principle is undermined by exceptions. Using Ibn Taymiyya's argument, write a short rebuttal showing that the exceptions (particulars) actually strengthen the universal principle when understood correctly.
