يقال: حركت يدي فتحرك الخاتم ، وكما يقال : حركت كفي فتحركت أصابعي»، وليست حركة الكف فاعلة لحركة الأصابع. وإن قدر أنها بعدها لم يُسلَّم اقترانهما في الزمان، بل تكون كأجزاء الزمان المتصل بعضُها ببعض، فإنه لا فصل بينها وإن كان الجزء الثاني متصلاً بالأول. كذلك أجزاء الحركة كحركة الظل ،وغيره كل جزء منها يحدث بعد الآخر وفي الزمان الذي يلي حركة الجزء الأول. فأما أن يقال: «الحركتان وجدتا معاً، وإحداهما فاعلة للأخرى فهذا باطل. وأيضاً فإن المعروف أنه إذا قيل حركتُ يدي فتحرك خاتمي أو المفتاح في كُمّي ونحو ذلك إذا تحرك بحركة تخصه، مثل أن يكون الخاتم مُلقي، فإذا حَرَّك يده عَلِق في اليد والخاتم يده فتحرك. وهذه الحركة بعد حركة اليد في الزمان وأما إذا كان الخاتم متصلاً بالأصبع ثابتاً فيها، وإنما يتحرك كما تتحرك الأصابع، فالمحرك للجميع واحد. ولو كان الإنسان نائماً أو ميتاً فرَفَع رجُلٌ يده وفيها الخاتم لكانت أيضاً متحركة بحركة اليد، وهي حركة واحدة شملت الجميعَ لاتصال بعضه ببعض. ليس هنا حركتان إحداهما سبب الأخرى، فضلاً عن أن تكون فاعلة لها. ومن قال في مثل هذا : «حركتُ يده فتحرك خاتمه فإنما هو كقوله فتحركت «اليد». فإنما يريد بذلك أن الحركة شملت الجميع، ولم يرد بذلك أن حركة اليد كانت سبباً للحركة الأولى. ولا نعرف عاقلاً يقول هذا ويقصد هذا. وإن قدر أنه وجد فليس قوله حجة على سائر العقلاء. ومن رفع يد ميت أو نائم وفيها خاتم لم تكن حركةُ بعض ذلك سبباً لبعض، بل الجميع موجود في زمان واحد لفاعل واحد وسبب واحد . فبطل أن يكون في الوجود سبب يقارن مسببه في الزمان، بل لا يكون إلا قبله. لا يقارن السبب مسببه فكيف بالفاعل المستقل؟ وإنما الذي يقارن الشيء في الزمان شرطه . وتقدم الواحد على في الزمان الاثنين هو من هذا الباب، لا من باب تقدم الشرط على المشروط . وحركة وحركة الخاتم مع اليد هي من باب المشروط مع الشرط، ليست من باب المفعول مع الفاعل . ولكن لفظ «العلة)) فيه إجمال . وكذلك الصوت مع الحركة فإن الصوت يحدث عقب الحركة، وغايته أن يكون معها كالجزء الثاني من الحركة مع الأول والحركة المتصلة، والزمان المتصل، ليس بعضه مع بعض في الزمان فغاية الصوت مع الحركة أن تكون كذلك. وكذلك الشعاع ظهور الشمس، مع أن الشمس ليست فاعلة للشعاع، بل الشعاع يحدث في الأرض إذا قابل الشمس ما ينعكس الشعاعُ عليه. وكذلك الحركة ليست هي الفاعلة للصوت ولكن الشمس شرط في الشعاع والحركة شرط في الصوت . وأما فاعل يُبدع مفعوله ويكون مقارناً له في الزمان فهذا لا يوجد قط . ولكن لفظ (العلة» فيه إجمال. فـ العلة الفاعلة شيء، و آخر. والشرط قد يقارن المشروط في زمانه بخلاف الفاعل فإنه لا بد أن يتقدم فعله هي شرط
According to the majority of rational people, there is no cause and effect here; rather, knowledge is the attribute of being knowing. This is the doctrine of the majority of the speculative theologians of Ahl al-Sunna and innovation, which is the negation of states, so there is no cause nor effect. And if the effect is made the judgment that he is knowing and the report about him that he is knowing, then this may be posterior to knowledge. And according to the one who affirms the state, he says: They are neither existent nor non-existent, so they are not analogous to existential effects. And also these people say: The knower is the agent of the attribute of being knowing, and he is not the maker of the attribute. And this is not, according to him, from the category of existential influence like causes and causes. For his being knowing is necessary for knowledge by virtue of knowledge being knowledge; this is not like cutting the neck being a cause for death nor like eating being a cause for satiety, from the causes that Allah created. So how about the causes that servants make, like his saying: 'If you commit adultery, you are divorced'? Here he attached an event to an event and judged that the first is a cause for the second. So where is this from knowledge and the attribute of being knowing? They were only deceived by the equivocation in the word 'cause'. And similarly the philosophers: In all that they gave as examples, there is no efficient cause that coexists with its effect, like the hand and the ring in time. For the movement of the hand is not the agent of the movement of the ring; rather, the mover of both is one. But their movements are concomitant due to the connection of the ring to the hand, like the movement of one part of the hand with another, and as it is said: 'I moved my hand, so my ring moved,' and as it is said: 'I moved my palm, so my fingers moved,' and the movement of the palm is not the agent of the movement of the fingers. And if it is assumed that it is after it, their coexistence in time is not conceded; rather, they are like parts of continuous time some with others, for there is no separation between them even if the second part is connected to the first. Similarly, the parts of movement, like the movement of the shadow and other things, each part occurs after the other and in the time that follows the movement of the first part. As for saying: 'The two movements exist together, and one of them is the agent of the other,' this is false. And also it is known that when it is said: 'I moved my hand, so my ring moved,' or the key in my sleeve and the like, if it moves with a movement specific to it, such as the ring being loose, then when he moves his hand, it hangs on the hand and the ring moves. And this movement is after the movement of the hand in time. But if the ring is connected to the finger and fixed in it, and it only moves as the fingers move, then the mover of all is one. And if a person were asleep or dead and a man lifted his hand with the ring in it, it would also move with the movement of the hand, and it is a single movement that encompasses all due to the connection of some parts to others. There are not two movements here, one the cause of the other, let alone being the agent of it. And whoever says in such a case: 'I moved his hand, so his ring moved,' it is only like his saying 'so the hand moved.' He only means by that that the movement encompassed all, and he does not intend by that that the movement of the hand was a cause for the first movement. And we do not know any rational person who says this and means this. And if it is assumed that such a person exists, his saying is not proof against the rest of rational people. And whoever lifts the hand of a dead or sleeping person with a ring in it, the movement of some part of that is not a cause for another part; rather, all exists in one time from one agent and one cause. So it is false that there exists in existence a cause that coexists with its effect in time; rather, it is only before it. The cause does not coexist with its effect, so how about the independent agent? What coexists with something in time is its condition. And the precedence of one over the other in time is of this category, not from the category of the precedence of the condition over the conditioned. And the movement of the ring with the hand is from the category of the conditioned with the condition, not from the category of the effect with the agent. But the word 'cause' is ambiguous. Similarly, sound with movement: for sound occurs after movement, and at most it is with it like the second part of movement with the first, and continuous movement and continuous time, some part of it is not with another in time; so at most sound is with movement in this way. Similarly, the ray with the appearance of the sun, even though the sun is not the agent of the ray; rather, the ray occurs on the earth when the sun faces what reflects the ray. And movement is not the agent of sound; rather, the sun is a condition for the ray, and movement is a condition for sound. As for an agent that innovates its effect and is simultaneous with it in time, this does not exist at all. But the word 'cause' is ambiguous. So the efficient cause is one thing, and the condition is another. The condition may coexist with the conditioned in its time, unlike the agent, for it must precede its action.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophers' claim that an efficient cause can coexist temporally with its effect. Ibn Taymiyya begins by discussing the concept of causality among theologians, noting that most rational people (including Sunni and Mu'tazili theologians) reject the notion of cause and effect in the context of divine attributes—they deny 'states' (ahwal). He then turns to the philosophers' examples, such as the hand moving a ring, to argue that such cases do not involve efficient causation but rather concomitance (talazum) due to physical connection or a common mover. He distinguishes between efficient cause (al-illa al-fa'ila), which must precede its effect, and condition (shart), which can be simultaneous. He illustrates with examples from motion (hand and ring, palm and fingers) and sound (sound follows movement but is not caused by it). He concludes that the philosophers' examples actually demonstrate conditionality, not efficient causation, and that the term 'cause' is ambiguous. The passage is structured as a critique: first clarifying the theological position, then attacking the philosophers' examples, and finally establishing the necessary temporal precedence of the efficient cause.

Key Takeaway

The Hand-Ring Example: Concomitance Not Efficient Causation
Ibn Taymiyya examines the hand-ring example to argue it involves concomitance, not efficient causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Ibn Taymiyya's refutation of the hand-ring example and identify why it does not demonstrate simultaneous efficient causation.

Core Idea
Simultaneous motion of hand and ring is due to a common mover, not efficient causality.

In the previous lesson, we saw the theological background. Now Ibn Taymiyya tackles the philosophers' favorite example: the hand moving a ring. He argues that the hand's movement is not the efficient cause of the ring's movement; rather, both are moved by the same agent, and the ring's movement is concomitant due to physical connection. This lesson dissects his analysis and shows the logical error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
What exactly do the philosophers say about the hand and ring? They claim the hand's movement is the efficient cause of the ring's movement, and they occur simultaneously.
Ibn Taymiyya quotes: 'I moved my hand, so my ring moved.'
2
Distinguish two scenarios
Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes when the ring is loose vs. fixed. In which case is there a temporal sequence? In which case is there a single movement?
Loose ring: ring moves after hand. Fixed ring: single movement from the same agent.
3
Identify the real cause
In the fixed ring case, what is the actual cause of the ring's movement? Is it the hand's movement or the person's intention?
The person is the mover of both; the hand and ring are moved by one agent.
4
Apply the cause-condition distinction
Ibn Taymiyya says this is an example of condition (shart) not efficient cause. The hand's movement is a condition for the ring's movement, not a cause. Why?
Because if the hand were not connected, the ring would not move; but the moving agent is the same.
5
Generalize to other examples
Ibn Taymiyya also discusses sound and movement, and sun and ray. Show how each fits the condition model.
Sound occurs after movement; ray occurs when sun faces an object. Both are conditions, not efficient causes.

يقال: حركت يدي فتحرك الخاتم ، وكما يقال : حركت كفي فتحركت أصابعي»، وليست حركة الكف فاعلة لحركة الأصابع. وإن قدر أنها بعدها لم يُسلَّم اقترانهما في الزمان، بل تكون كأجزاء الزمان المتصل بعضُها ببعض، فاعلة للأخرى فهذا باطل. وأيضاً فإن المعروف أنه إذا قيل حركتُ يدي فتحرك خاتمي أو المفتاح في كُمّي ونحو ذلك إذا تحرك بحركة تخصه، مثل أن يكون الخاتم مُلقي، فإذا حَرَّك يده عَلِق في اليد والخاتم يده كقوله فتحركت «اليد». فإنما يريد بذلك أن الحركة شملت الجميع، ولم يرد بذلك أن حركة اليد كانت سبباً للحركة الأولى. ولا نعرف عاقلاً يقول هذا ويقصد هذا. وإن قدر أنه وجد فليس قوله حجة على سائر العقلاء. ومن رفع يد ميت أو نائم وفيها خاتم لم تكن حركةُ بعض ذلك سبباً لبعض، بل الجميع موجود في زمان واحد لفاعل واحد وسبب واحد . فبطل أن يكون في الوجود سبب يقارن مسببه في الزمان، بل لا يكون إلا قبله. لا يقارن السبب مسببه فكيف بالفاعل المستقل؟ وإنما الذي يقارن الشيء في الزمان شرطه . وتقدم الواحد على في الزمان الاثنين هو من هذا الباب، لا من باب تقدم الشرط على المشروط . وحركة وحركة الخاتم مع اليد هي من باب المشروط مع الشرط، ليست من باب المفعول مع الفاعل . ولكن لفظ «العلة)) فيه إجمال . وكذلك الصوت مع الحركة فإن الصوت يحدث عقب الحركة، وغايته أن يكون معها كالجزء الثاني من الحركة مع الأول والحركة المتصلة، والزمان المتصل، ليس بعضه مع بعض في الزمان فغاية الصوت مع الحركة أن تكون كذلك. وكذلك الشعاع ظهور الشمس، مع أن الشمس ليست فاعلة للشعاع، بل الشعاع يحدث في الأرض إذا قابل الشمس ما ينعكس الشعاعُ عليه. وكذلك الحركة ليست هي الفاعلة للصوت ولكن الشمس شرط في الشعاع والحركة شرط في الصوت . وأما فاعل يُبدع مفعوله ويكون مقارناً له في الزمان فهذا لا يوجد قط . ولكن لفظ (العلة» فيه إجمال. فـ العلة الفاعلة شيء، و آخر. والشرط قد يقارن المشروط في زمانه بخلاف الفاعل فإنه لا بد أن يتقدم فعله هي شرط
According to the majority of rational people, there is no cause and effect here; rather, knowledge is the attribute of being knowing. This is the doctrine of the majority of the speculative theologians of Ahl al-Sunna and innovation, which is the negation of states, so there is no cause nor effect. And if the effect is made the judgment that he is knowing and the report about him that he is knowing, then this may be posterior to knowledge. And according to the one who affirms the state, he says: They are neither existent nor non-existent, so they are not analogous to existential effects. And also these people say: The knower is the agent of the attribute of being knowing, and he is not the maker of the attribute. And this is not, according to him, from the category of existential influence like causes and causes. For his being knowing is necessary for knowledge by virtue of knowledge being knowledge; this is not like cutting the neck being a cause for death nor like eating being a cause for satiety, from the causes that Allah created. So how about the causes that servants make, like his saying: 'If you commit adultery, you are divorced'? Here he attached an event to an event and judged that the first is a cause for the second. So where is this from knowledge and the attribute of being knowing? They were only deceived by the equivocation in the word 'cause'. And similarly the philosophers: In all that they gave as examples, there is no efficient cause that coexists with its effect, like the hand and the ring in time. For the movement of the hand is not the agent of the movement of the ring; rather, the mover of both is one. But their movements are concomitant due to the connection of the ring to the hand, like the movement of one part of the hand with another, and as it is said: 'I moved my hand, so my ring moved,' and as it is said: 'I moved my palm, so my fingers moved,' and the movement of the palm is not the agent of the movement of the fingers. And if it is assumed that it is after it, their coexistence in time is not conceded; rather, they are like parts of continuous time some with others, for there is no separation between them even if the second part is connected to the first. Similarly, the parts of movement, like the movement of the shadow and other things, each part occurs after the other and in the time that follows the movement of the first part. As for saying: 'The two movements exist together, and one of them is the agent of the other,' this is false. And also it is known that when it is said: 'I moved my hand, so my ring moved,' or the key in my sleeve and the like, if it moves with a movement specific to it, such as the ring being loose, then when he moves his hand, it hangs on the hand and the ring moves. And this movement is after the movement of the hand in time. But if the ring is connected to the finger and fixed in it, and it only moves as the fingers move, then the mover of all is one. And if a person were asleep or dead and a man lifted his hand with the ring in it, it would also move with the movement of the hand, and it is a single movement that encompasses all due to the connection of some parts to others. There are not two movements here, one the cause of the other, let alone being the agent of it. And whoever says in such a case: 'I moved his hand, so his ring moved,' it is only like his saying 'so the hand moved.' He only means by that that the movement encompassed all, and he does not intend by that that the movement of the hand was a cause for the first movement. And we do not know any rational person who says this and means this. And if it is assumed that such a person exists, his saying is not proof against the rest of rational people. And whoever lifts the hand of a dead or sleeping person with a ring in it, the movement of some part of that is not a cause for another part; rather, all exists in one time from one agent and one cause. So it is false that there exists in existence a cause that coexists with its effect in time; rather, it is only before it. The cause does not coexist with its effect, so how about the independent agent? What coexists with something in time is its condition. And the precedence of one over the other in time is of this category, not from the category of the precedence of the condition over the conditioned. And the movement of the ring with the hand is from the category of the conditioned with the condition, not from the category of the effect with the agent. But the word 'cause' is ambiguous. Similarly, sound with movement: for sound occurs after movement, and at most it is with it like the second part of movement with the first, and continuous movement and continuous time, some part of it is not with another in time; so at most sound is with movement in this way. Similarly, the ray with the appearance of the sun, even though the sun is not the agent of the ray; rather, the ray occurs on the earth when the sun faces what reflects the ray. And movement is not the agent of sound; rather, the sun is a condition for the ray, and movement is a condition for sound. As for an agent that innovates its effect and is simultaneous with it in time, this does not exist at all. But the word 'cause' is ambiguous. So the efficient cause is one thing, and the condition is another. The condition may coexist with the conditioned in its time, unlike the agent, for it must precede its action.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophers' claim that an efficient cause can coexist temporally with its effect. Ibn Taymiyya begins by discussing the concept of causality among theologians, noting that most rational people (including Sunni and Mu'tazili theologians) reject the notion of cause and effect in the context of divine attributes—they deny 'states' (ahwal). He then turns to the philosophers' examples, such as the hand moving a ring, to argue that such cases do not involve efficient causation but rather concomitance (talazum) due to physical connection or a common mover. He distinguishes between efficient cause (al-illa al-fa'ila), which must precede its effect, and condition (shart), which can be simultaneous. He illustrates with examples from motion (hand and ring, palm and fingers) and sound (sound follows movement but is not caused by it). He concludes that the philosophers' examples actually demonstrate conditionality, not efficient causation, and that the term 'cause' is ambiguous. The passage is structured as a critique: first clarifying the theological position, then attacking the philosophers' examples, and finally establishing the necessary temporal precedence of the efficient cause.

Ibn Taymiyya on Redefining 'Muhdath'
Author critiques philosophers' redefinition of 'muhdath' as essentially caused rather than temporally originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the philosophers' redefinition of 'muhdath' and evaluate its linguistic and logical force.

Core Idea
The philosophers changed 'muhdath' from 'temporally originated' to 'essentially caused' – but language and fitra reject this.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya identifies a crucial terminological distortion that underpins many philosophical errors. He argues that the philosophers have corrupted the meaning of 'muhdath' (temporally originated) to suit their metaphysical system. Learning to detect such shifts is essential for anyone engaged in Islamic theology and logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original meaning of 'muhdath'
Find the passage where Ibn Taymiyya states what 'muhdath' truly means: something that comes into existence after non-existence. Note that he appeals to the consensus of 'rational people' and the messengers.
The original meaning is tied to temporal origination, not mere causation.
2
State the philosophers' redefinition
Locate where the philosophers say 'muhdath' means 'essentially caused' (ma'lul). They apply it to all contingent beings, including eternal effects.
This redefinition allows them to call the eternal world 'muhdath' in a non-temporal sense.
3
Examine Ibn Taymiyya's evidence for the original meaning
He cites universal linguistic custom, the reports of the messengers, and the intellect. He says no rational person in any language calls the eternal 'muhdath'.
His appeal is to pre-philosophical common sense and revelation.
4
Understand why this is a distortion
The redefinition applies a term reserved for temporal origination to what is eternal. This is 'tahrif al-kalim 'an mawadi'ihi' – distorting words from their proper places.
It is not a neutral terminological shift but an illegitimate extension.
5
Evaluate the theological stakes
If 'muhdath' can mean 'eternally caused', then the proof of the world's temporal origination (huduth) fails. Ibn Taymiyya blocks this by fixing the meaning.
The debate over whether the world is eternal or created hangs on this definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كون كل مخلوق محدثاً. على المعيَّن. وإذا قدر أنه لم يزل فاعلاً فكل جزء من أجزاء الفعل مسبوق بجزء آخر. وإن كان نوع الفعل لم يزل فلا يتصوّر أن يكون فعله أو مفعوله معيّناً مع الله أزلاً وأبداً. وإن قيل : إنه لم يزل فاعلاً بمشيئته، فدوام نوع الفعل شيء، ودوام الفعل المعين والمفعول المعين شيء آخر. والذي أخبرَتْ به الرسلُ، ودلت عليه العقولُ، واتفق عليه جماهير العقلاء من الأولين والآخرين أن الله خالق كل شيء، وأن كل ما سواه فهو مخلوق له. مخلوق محدَث مسبوق بالعدم. وأما تغيير هؤلاء للفظ «المحدث» وقولهم: إنا نقول إنه محدث حدوثاً ذاتياً بمعنى أنه «معلول» فهذا من تحريف الكلم عن مواضعه. فإن «المحدث» معلوم أنه قد كان بعد أن لم يكن، وأنه مفعولُ أحدثه محدث إحداثاً. وما لم يزل ولا يزال فلا أحد يسميه من العقلاء في لغة من اللغات محدثاً، بل ولا يقول أحد العقلاء أنه «مفعول» «مصنوع» «مخلوق»، ولا يقول أحد أنه «ممكن يمكن وجوده ويمكن عدمه إلا هذه الشرذمة من الفلاسفة.
That every creature is originated (muhdath). Upon the specific. And if it is supposed that He is always acting, then every part of the act is preceded by another part. And if the type of act is eternal, then it is inconceivable that His act or effect be specific with God eternally and forever. And if it is said that He is always acting by His will, then the perpetuity of the type of act is one thing, and the perpetuity of the specific act and the specific effect is another. What the messengers informed, and the intellects indicated, and the majority of rational people from the first and later generations agreed upon is that God is the Creator of everything, and that everything other than Him is created by Him: a created being, originated, preceded by non-existence. As for these people's alteration of the word 'al-muhdath' and their saying, 'We say it is originated with an essential origination meaning it is an effect,' this is distortion of words from their proper places. For 'al-muhdath' is known to be that which came into being after not being, and that it is an effect produced by a producer producing it. That which has always been and will always be, no rational person in any language calls it 'muhdath', nor does any rational person say it is 'an effect', 'manufactured', 'created', nor does anyone say it is 'possible, whose existence and non-existence are possible' except this sect of philosophers.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the philosophers' redefinition of the term 'muhdath'. The author begins by stating the premise that every creature is muhdath (temporally originated). He then considers a hypothetical: even if God were always acting, the specific act and effect are not eternal. The messengers and intellects agree that everything other than God is created and preceded by non-existence. The philosophers, however, have changed the meaning of 'muhdath' from 'temporally originated' to 'essentially caused' (ma'lul). The author argues this is a distortion of language, because 'muhdath' universally means something that came into being after non-existence. No rational person would call the eternal being 'muhdath' or 'created' or 'possible'. The philosophers' redefinition is a deviation from common linguistic usage and the consensus of rational people.

B) They redefine it as 'essentially caused' (ma'lul).
C) They reject the term entirely.
D) They apply it only to the eternal being.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the philosophers' redefinition of 'muhdath' from 'temporally originated' to 'essentially caused' is a linguistic distortion that undermines the correct understanding of creation and causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a contemporary debate in usul al-fiqh where a key term (e.g., 'qiyas', 'ijma') is redefined by one school. Apply Ibn Taymiyya's method: compare the original linguistic meaning with the redefinition, and assess whether the redefinition is a distortion.
?
Take the following claim from a philosopher: 'The world is muhdath because it is caused.' Using Ibn Taymiyya's argument, explain why this claim is problematic.

The Inconsistency of Logicians on Sensory and Recurrent Knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya refutes logicians' restriction of burhan premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument from parity against the logicians' claim that recurrent reports cannot serve as premises for demonstrative proof (burhan).

Core Idea
If sensory knowledge is too private for proof, then recurrent reports are even more so—but logicians accept the former and reject the latter.

Ibn Taymiyya catches the logicians in a contradiction. They claim that mutawatirat (recurrent reports) cannot provide universal proof because they are not shared by all, yet they happily accept sensory knowledge (hissiyat) which is even more private. In this lesson, you will trace his argument from parity and see how he exposes the inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
The logicians assert that mutawatirat, mujarrabat, and hadsiyyat cannot be premises for burhan because they are specific to those who know them and not universally shared.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's parallel
He draws an analogy: if that criterion holds, then sensory knowledge (smelled, tasted, touched) is even more specific, so it should also be excluded.
3
Compare shareability of sensory vs. recurrent reports
Recurrent reports are transmitted by many and reach vast audiences, while sensory perceptions are inherently private to the individual experiencer.
4
Assess the inconsistency
The logicians accept sensory knowledge in demonstration (e.g., in natural science) but reject mutawatirat; Ibn Taymiyya shows this is arbitrary and self-defeating.
5
Respond to the genus counterargument
If the logicians argue that sensory knowledge can be shared in genus, Ibn Taymiyya replies that the same applies to mutawatirat and mujarrabat, closing their escape route.

هؤلاء ما لم يتواتر عند غيرهم ولم يجربوه. ولكن قد يتفقان في الجنس، كما يجرب قوم بعض الأدوية ويجرب الآخرون جنس تلك الأدوية فيتفق في معرفة الجنس، لا معرفة عين المجرّب. ثم هم مع هذا، يقولون في المنطق: إن المتواترات، والمجربات، لا يقوم بها والحدسيات تختص بمن عَلِمها، فلا يقوم منها برهان على غيره. فيقال لهم: وكذلك برهان على المشمومات والمَذُوقات والملموسات. بل اشتراك الناس في المتواترات أكثر. فإن الخبر المتواتر ينقله عدد كثير فيكثر السامعون له ويشتركون في سماعه العدد من الكثير، لا سيما إذا كان العدد الكثير مئين وألوفاً فبطائفة من هؤلاء يحصل العلم المتواتر. فإذا نَقَل هؤلاء القوم، وهؤلاء القوم، وهؤلاء لقوم، حصل العلم المتواتر لأمم لا يُحصي عددهم إلا الله بخلاف ما يدرك بالحواس فإنه يختص بمن أحسه. فإذا قال «رأيتُ» أو «سمعتُ» أو «ذقتُ» أو «لمستُ» أو «شممتُ» فكيف يمكنه أن يقيم من برهاناً على غيره؟ ولو قدر أنه شاركه في تلك الحسيات عدد فلا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون غيرُهم أحَسَّها. ولا يمكن علمها لمن لم يُحِسَّها إلا بطريق الخبر. فإذا ادعوا أن «الخبر المتواتر مختص بالحسيات، فلا يقوم به البرهان على المنازع» فالحسيات أعظم اختصاصاً، فيلزم أن لا يقوم بها برهان على المنازع وليس في الحسيات أكثر اشتراكاً من «الرؤية»، فإن الناس يشتركون في رؤية الأنوار العلوية، كالشمس والقمر والكواكب، ولكن مواد البرهان لا يختص بذلك. وإن قالوا الحسيات تحصل بالاشتراك في جنسها، كاشتراك الناس في قد تحصل معرفة الألوان والطعوم والروائح والحرارة والبرودة، وإن لم يكن عين ما أحسه بالاشتراك هؤلاء هو عين ما أحسه هؤلاء، لكن اشتركا في الجنس قيل: والمتواترات والمجربات قد يشتركون في جنسها كما تقدم. بل وجود الشبع والرّي عقيب الأكل والشرب هو من المجربات، والناس يشتركون في جنسه. وكذلك وجود اللذة بذلك، وبالجماع وغير ذلك، بما إذا فعله الإنسان وجد عقيبه أثراً من الآثار، ثم يتكرر ذلك حتى يَعْلَم أن ذاك سبب هذا الأثر، فهذا هو التجربيات. المقام الرابع - الوجه الثامن: كون الحسيات لا يقوم بها برهان على المنازع والقضايا الظنية أصلها التجربيات، وهو من هذا الباب. فإن المراد أنه إذا استعمل الدواء الفلاني وجد زوال المرض أو حصل به ألم المرض. فوجود المرض بهذا و وجود زواله بهذا هو من التجربيات وكذلك الآلام واللذات التي تحصل بالمشمومات والمسموعات والمرئيات والملموسات فإن الحس ينال كذا ويسمع هذا، ويذوق هذا، ويلمس هذا ثم وجود اللذة في النفس هو من الوجديات المعلومة بالحس الباطن، وهو من جنس الحسيات الظاهرة. وأما الاعتقاد الكلي القائم في النفس من أن هذا الجنس يحصل به اللذة، وهذا التجربيات مركبة من الجنس يحصل به الألم فهذا من التجربيات، إذ الحسيات الظاهرة والباطنة ليس فيها الحس والعقل شيء كلي. فالقضاء الكلي الذي يقوم بالقلب هو مركب من الحس والعقل، وهو التجربيات، كما في اعتقاد حصول الشبع والري بما يعرف من المأكولات والمشروبات والموتِ والمرض بما يعرف من السموم القتالة والأسباب الممرضة، وزوال ذلك بالأسباب المعروفة وكل هذا من القضايا التجربية. فالحس به تعرف الأمور المعينة، ثم إذا تكررت مرة بعد مرة أدرك العقل أن هذا بسبب القدر المشترك الكلي، فقضى قضاء كلياً أن هذا يورث اللذة الفُلانية، وهذا يورث الألم الفلاني. والحَدْسِيّات هي كذلك. فبالحس يعرف أعيانها، ثم يتكرر فيعلم بالعقل القدرُ المشترك. لكن ذلك التكرر لا يكون بفعل الإنسان، بل هو كما يعرف من الأمور الحدسيات السماوية، مثل أن يرى اختلاف أنوار القمر عند اختلاف مقابلته للشمس، فَيَحْدِسُ أن ضوءه مستفاد منها. ومثل ما يرى الثوابت لا تختلف حركتها بالطول والعرض، بل حركتها حركة واحدة فيُحْدِس أن فلكها واحد، وإلا فيجوز أن يكون لكل واحد فلكاً حركته مثل حركة الآخر. ومثل ما يرى اختلاف حركات السبعة، فيحدس عن اختلاف أفلاكها. وهذا يسمى في اللغة تجربيات. وكثير منهم أيضاً يسميه «تجربيات»، ولا يجعل قسماً غير «المجربات». وبالجملة الأمورُ العادية، سواء كان سبب العادة إرادةً نفسانية أو قوة طبيعية، كون العاديات من باب التجربيات فالعلم بكونها كلية هو من التجربيات، أو الحدسيات - إن جعلت نوعاً آخر.
These are those that have not been recurrently transmitted to others nor have they been experienced. But they may agree in genus, just as some people experiment with certain medicines and others experiment with the genus of those medicines, so they agree in knowledge of the genus, not knowledge of the specific thing experimented upon. Then despite this, they say in logic: 'Indeed, recurrent reports and experiential data do not provide a proof, and intuitive matters are specific to those who know them, so a proof cannot be established from them for others.' So it is said to them: Likewise, a proof cannot be established upon smelled, tasted, and touched things. Rather, people's sharing in recurrent reports is greater. For the recurrently transmitted report is transmitted by a large number, so the listeners to it become many and they share in hearing it from many people, especially if the large number is in hundreds and thousands, so by a group of these people recurrent knowledge is obtained. So if this group transmits, and this group, and this group, recurrent knowledge is obtained for nations whose number no one knows except God, unlike what is perceived by the senses, for it is specific to the one who sensed it. So if he says 'I saw' or 'I heard' or 'I tasted' or 'I touched' or 'I smelled', how can he establish a proof for others? And if one supposes that a number shared in those sensory perceptions, it does not follow that others have sensed them. Nor can they be known by those who have not sensed them except by way of report. So if they claim that 'recurrent report is specific to sensory matters, so a proof cannot be established by it for the opponent,' then sensory matters are even more specific, so it follows that a proof cannot be established by them for the opponent. And there is nothing in sensory matters more shared than 'sight', for people share in seeing celestial lights like the sun, moon, and stars, but the materials of proof are not specific to that. And if they say that sensory matters are obtained by sharing in their genus, like people's sharing in the fact that knowledge of colors, tastes, smells, heat and cold may be obtained, even if the specific thing sensed by these is not the specific thing sensed by those, but they share in the genus, it is said: Recurrent reports and experiential data may be shared in their genus as previously mentioned. Rather, the occurrence of satiety and thirst after eating and drinking is from experiential data, and people share in its genus. Likewise the occurrence of pleasure from that, and from intercourse and other things, such that when a person does it he finds an effect after it, then it is repeated until he knows that that is the cause of this effect, this is experiential data. The fourth station - the eighth aspect: That sensory matters do not provide a proof for the opponent. And speculative propositions have their origin in experiential data, and it is from this chapter. For the meaning is that when he uses such-and-such a medicine, he finds the removal of illness or the occurrence of pain from illness. So the occurrence of illness by this and the occurrence of its removal by this is from experiential data. Likewise the pains and pleasures that occur through smells, sounds, sights, and touches, for the sense obtains such, hears this, tastes this, touches this, then the occurrence of pleasure in the soul is from subjective experiences known by inner sense, and it is of the genus of external sensory matters. As for the universal belief established in the soul that this genus brings pleasure, and this brings pain, this is from experiential data, for external and internal sensory matters do not contain anything universal by sense and intellect. So the universal judgment that arises in the heart is composed of sense and intellect, and it is experiential data, as in the belief of obtaining satiety and thirst from what is known of foods and drinks, and death and illness from what is known of deadly poisons and harmful causes, and their removal by known causes, and all of this is from experiential propositions. For by sense, particular things are known, then when they are repeated time after time, the intellect perceives that this is due to the universal common factor, so it makes a universal judgment that this brings such-and-such pleasure, and this brings such-and-such pain. And intuitive matters are likewise. For by sense their particulars are known, then they are repeated and the intellect knows the common factor. But that repetition does not occur by human action; rather it is like what is known of celestial intuitive matters, such as seeing the difference in the moon's light according to its differing opposition to the sun, so one intuits that its light is derived from it. And like seeing that the fixed stars do not differ in their motion in longitude and latitude, but rather their motion is one motion, so one intuits that their sphere is one, otherwise it would be possible for each to have a sphere with a motion like the other's motion. And like seeing the difference in the motions of the seven planets, so one intuits the difference of their spheres. And this is called in language 'experiential data'. And many of them also call it 'experiential data', and do not make it a division other than 'experiential data'. In summary, customary matters, whether the cause of the custom is a psychic will or a natural power, that customary matters are from the category of experiential data, so the knowledge that they are universal is from experiential data, or from intuitive data - if made another type.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's *Radd Mantiqiyyin* engages in a critical refutation of the logicians' epistemological claims. The target is the assertion found in Aristotelian logic that certain types of propositions—specifically, recurrent reports (*mutawatirat*), experiential data (*mujarribat*), and intuitive judgments (*hadsiyat*)—cannot serve as premises for a demonstration (*burhan*) that commands universal assent, because they are either too particular or depend on the individual experiencer. Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that the logicians themselves admit that these types of knowledge are valid for those who possess them, but they claim that a *burhan* cannot be built from them for others. He then draws a parallel: if that is the case, then it applies even more strongly to sensory knowledge (*hissiyat*), which is even more particular. Yet the logicians themselves use sensory knowledge (e.g., in natural science) without objection. He argues that recurrent reports are actually more widely shared than sensory perceptions, because a large number of people transmit and receive them, making them publicly known, while sensory perceptions are private. Thus the logicians' position is inconsistent: they reject recurrent reports as proof but implicitly accept sensory experience, which is even less shareable. Ibn Taymiyya then addresses a possible counter: that sensory knowledge can be shared in genus (e.g., everyone knows colors, even if not the same specific color). He responds that the same is true of recurrent reports and experiential data—people share in the genus of such knowledge, as when they know that eating causes satiety or that certain medicines cure disease. He then elaborates on how experiential knowledge arises: through repeated observation of particular events, the intellect perceives a universal causal connection. This composite of sense and intellect yields universal judgments, which are the basis of empirical propositions (*qadaya tajribiyya*). Intuitive knowledge (*hadsiyat*) follows a similar pattern, but without deliberate human repetition; examples include astronomical insights like deducing that moonlight comes from the sun. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that customary regularities, whether psychological or natural, are ultimately grounded in experiential data, so they can serve as premises for reasoning. Throughout, he undercuts the logicians' attempt to restrict the materials of demonstration, defending a broader empiricist epistemology rooted in common human experience.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya's argument in this passage, which of the following is true?
A) Recurrent reports are more private than sensory knowledge.
B) Sensory knowledge is more universally shared than recurrent reports.
C) If recurrent reports cannot serve as proof, then sensory knowledge also cannot, given the logicians' own criterion.
D) The logicians consistently reject both sensory and recurrent knowledge as premises.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses parity to show that if the logicians' standard for burhan is universal shareability, then sensory knowledge fails that standard even more than mutawatirat, so the logicians' position is incoherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the logicians' claim that only necessary and universal propositions can be premises for burhan, construct a counter-argument using Ibn Taymiyya's method of parity: show that they implicitly accept contingent or particular premises in other contexts, exposing inconsistency.
?
Analyze the following statement from a modern scholar: 'Empirical science cannot yield certainty, so it cannot be used in theological proof.' Identify which of Ibn Taymiyya's moves would apply to this claim and why.

The Composition of Experiential Knowledge (Mujarrabat)
Ibn Taymiyya explains the epistemological structure of experiential knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how experiential knowledge arises from the combination of repeated sense perceptions and intellectual inference, and why Ibn Taymiyya considers it a legitimate source of universal premises for demonstration.

Core Idea
Experiential knowledge is not mere sense-data; it is a universal judgment formed by the intellect from repeated particular observations.

Ibn Taymiyya goes beyond the negative critique to offer a positive account of how experiential knowledge (mujarrabat) works. He explains that it is composed of sense (hiss) and intellect ('aql), and that universal causal judgments arise from repeated observation. This lesson unpacks that process and shows why he classifies it as a valid source of demonstrative premises.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of experiential knowledge
Find where Ibn Taymiyya says experiential knowledge is composed of sense and intellect.
2
Identify the role of sense
Sense perceives particular instances (e.g., drinking water leads to satiety in a specific case).
3
Identify the role of intellect
After repetition, the intellect grasps the universal common factor (e.g., drinking water in general causes satiety).
4
Note the examples
He gives examples: eating causes satiety, poisons cause death, medicines cure.
5
Contrast with logicians
The logicians deny these as burhan premises; Ibn Taymiyya argues they are universal and shareable.

إن جعلت نوعاً آخر.
These are those that have not been recurrently transmitted to others nor have they been experienced. But they may agree in genus, just as some people experiment with certain medicines and others experiment with the genus of those medicines, so they agree in knowledge of the genus, not knowledge of the specific thing experimented upon. Then despite this, they say in logic: 'Indeed, recurrent reports and experiential data do not provide a proof, and intuitive matters are specific to those who know them, so a proof cannot be established from them for others.' So it is said to them: Likewise, a proof cannot be established upon smelled, tasted, and touched things. Rather, people's sharing in recurrent reports is greater. For the recurrently transmitted report is transmitted by a large number, so the listeners to it become many and they share in hearing it from many people, especially if the large number is in hundreds and thousands, so by a group of these people recurrent knowledge is obtained. So if this group transmits, and this group, and this group, recurrent knowledge is obtained for nations whose number no one knows except God, unlike what is perceived by the senses, for it is specific to the one who sensed it. So if he says 'I saw' or 'I heard' or 'I tasted' or 'I touched' or 'I smelled', how can he establish a proof for others? And if one supposes that a number shared in those sensory perceptions, it does not follow that others have sensed them. Nor can they be known by those who have not sensed them except by way of report. So if they claim that 'recurrent report is specific to sensory matters, so a proof cannot be established by it for the opponent,' then sensory matters are even more specific, so it follows that a proof cannot be established by them for the opponent. And there is nothing in sensory matters more shared than 'sight', for people share in seeing celestial lights like the sun, moon, and stars, but the materials of proof are not specific to that. And if they say that sensory matters are obtained by sharing in their genus, like people's sharing in the fact that knowledge of colors, tastes, smells, heat and cold may be obtained, even if the specific thing sensed by these is not the specific thing sensed by those, but they share in the genus, it is said: Recurrent reports and experiential data may be shared in their genus as previously mentioned. Rather, the occurrence of satiety and thirst after eating and drinking is from experiential data, and people share in its genus. Likewise the occurrence of pleasure from that, and from intercourse and other things, such that when a person does it he finds an effect after it, then it is repeated until he knows that that is the cause of this effect, this is experiential data. The fourth station - the eighth aspect: That sensory matters do not provide a proof for the opponent. And speculative propositions have their origin in experiential data, and it is from this chapter. For the meaning is that when he uses such-and-such a medicine, he finds the removal of illness or the occurrence of pain from illness. So the occurrence of illness by this and the occurrence of its removal by this is from experiential data. Likewise the pains and pleasures that occur through smells, sounds, sights, and touches, for the sense obtains such, hears this, tastes this, touches this, then the occurrence of pleasure in the soul is from subjective experiences known by inner sense, and it is of the genus of external sensory matters. As for the universal belief established in the soul that this genus brings pleasure, and this brings pain, this is from experiential data, for external and internal sensory matters do not contain anything universal by sense and intellect. So the universal judgment that arises in the heart is composed of sense and intellect, and it is experiential data, as in the belief of obtaining satiety and thirst from what is known of foods and drinks, and death and illness from what is known of deadly poisons and harmful causes, and their removal by known causes, and all of this is from experiential propositions. For by sense, particular things are known, then when they are repeated time after time, the intellect perceives that this is due to the universal common factor, so it makes a universal judgment that this brings such-and-such pleasure, and this brings such-and-such pain. And intuitive matters are likewise. For by sense their particulars are known, then they are repeated and the intellect knows the common factor. But that repetition does not occur by human action; rather it is like what is known of celestial intuitive matters, such as seeing the difference in the moon's light according to its differing opposition to the sun, so one intuits that its light is derived from it. And like seeing that the fixed stars do not differ in their motion in longitude and latitude, but rather their motion is one motion, so one intuits that their sphere is one, otherwise it would be possible for each to have a sphere with a motion like the other's motion. And like seeing the difference in the motions of the seven planets, so one intuits the difference of their spheres. And this is called in language 'experiential data'. And many of them also call it 'experiential data', and do not make it a division other than 'experiential data'. In summary, customary matters, whether the cause of the custom is a psychic will or a natural power, that customary matters are from the category of experiential data, so the knowledge that they are universal is from experiential data, or from intuitive data - if made another type.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's *Radd Mantiqiyyin* engages in a critical refutation of the logicians' epistemological claims. The target is the assertion found in Aristotelian logic that certain types of propositions—specifically, recurrent reports (*mutawatirat*), experiential data (*mujarribat*), and intuitive judgments (*hadsiyat*)—cannot serve as premises for a demonstration (*burhan*) that commands universal assent, because they are either too particular or depend on the individual experiencer. Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that the logicians themselves admit that these types of knowledge are valid for those who possess them, but they claim that a *burhan* cannot be built from them for others. He then draws a parallel: if that is the case, then it applies even more strongly to sensory knowledge (*hissiyat*), which is even more particular. Yet the logicians themselves use sensory knowledge (e.g., in natural science) without objection. He argues that recurrent reports are actually more widely shared than sensory perceptions, because a large number of people transmit and receive them, making them publicly known, while sensory perceptions are private. Thus the logicians' position is inconsistent: they reject recurrent reports as proof but implicitly accept sensory experience, which is even less shareable. Ibn Taymiyya then addresses a possible counter: that sensory knowledge can be shared in genus (e.g., everyone knows colors, even if not the same specific color). He responds that the same is true of recurrent reports and experiential data—people share in the genus of such knowledge, as when they know that eating causes satiety or that certain medicines cure disease. He then elaborates on how experiential knowledge arises: through repeated observation of particular events, the intellect perceives a universal causal connection. This composite of sense and intellect yields universal judgments, which are the basis of empirical propositions (*qadaya tajribiyya*). Intuitive knowledge (*hadsiyat*) follows a similar pattern, but without deliberate human repetition; examples include astronomical insights like deducing that moonlight comes from the sun. Ibn Taymiyya concludes that customary regularities, whether psychological or natural, are ultimately grounded in experiential data, so they can serve as premises for reasoning. Throughout, he undercuts the logicians' attempt to restrict the materials of demonstration, defending a broader empiricist epistemology rooted in common human experience.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how does the universal judgment of experiential knowledge arise?
A) Solely from repeated sense perceptions without intellect.
B) From the intellect alone, without sense experience.
C) From the combination of sense perception of particulars and intellectual grasp of the common factor after repetition.
D) From divine revelation only.

Key Takeaway
Experiential knowledge is a composite of sense and intellect: sense provides particular instances, intellect extracts the universal causal connection, making it a valid source of universal premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the example: 'Smoking causes lung cancer.' Using Ibn Taymiyya's account, explain how repeated observation (sense) combined with intellectual inference yields a universal judgment that can serve as a basis for a legal ruling (e.g., prohibition).
?
Identify the role of sense and intellect in the classical example: 'Fire burns.' How does this become a universal judgment?
